<b>He,</b> Rabba, <b>raised an objection to him</b> from another teaching of our mishna: With regard to one who <b>immersed without</b> intending to <b>assume a presumptive status of ritual purity,</b> it is <b>as if he has not immersed. What, is it not</b> teaching that it is <b>as if he had not immersed at all?</b>
Rav Naḥman rejected this proof as well: <b>No,</b> it means that it is <b>as if he has not immersed for tithes, but he is</b> considered to have <b>immersed for non-sacred</b> produce, for which no intention is necessary. The Gemara comments: <b>He,</b> i.e., Rabba, <b>thought</b> that Rav Naḥman <b>was</b> merely <b>refuting his</b> proof by saying that the wording of the mishna does not conclusively prove his case, but he did not actually think that the mishna should be understood differently. However, he subsequently <b>went</b> and <b>examined</b> the sources <b>and found that</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>was</b> explicitly <b>taught</b> in accordance with the opinion of Rav Naḥman: One who <b>immersed and</b> did not intend to <b>assume a presumptive status</b> of ritual purity <b>is prohibited from</b> eating <b>tithes, but is permitted to</b> eat <b>non-sacred</b> produce, even if he eats non-sacred produce only when ritually pure.
§ With regard to assuming a presumptive status of ritual purity, <b>Rabbi Elazar said:</b> If one <b>immersed</b> without any particular intention <b>and ascended</b> from his immersion, <b>he may assume a presumptive status</b> of ritual purity after his immersion <b>for whatever he wishes.</b> In his opinion, there is no need to have a definite intention in mind at the actual moment of immersion.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to this from the following <i>baraita</i>: In a case where one has immersed and is ascending, <b>and one of his feet is still in the water, if he</b> had originally intended to <b>assume presumptive status</b> of ritual purity <b>for a minor matter, he may</b> still intend to <b>assume presumptive status for a major matter.</b> But if he has fully <b>ascended, he may no longer</b> intend to <b>assume a presumptive status</b> for any other matter.
The Gemara infers from this <i>baraita</i>: <b>What, is it not</b> teaching that if one has ascended <b>he may not</b> intend to <b>assume a presumptive status</b> of ritual purity <b>at all,</b> which proves that one may do so only if he is still at least partially in the water?
The Gemara rejects this inference: <b>No,</b> it should be understood as follows: If <b>he is still</b> in the water, then although <b>he</b> previously intended to <b>assume a presumptive status</b> of ritual purity for a minor matter, <b>he may</b> now intend to <b>assume a presumptive status</b> of ritual purity for whatever purpose he wishes, since one can adjust his intention during his immersion. Once he has already <b>ascended, if he did not</b> intend to <b>assume a presumptive status</b> of ritual purity at all, but immersed without any intention, <b>he may</b> intend to <b>assume a presumptive status</b> of ritual purity for whatever he wishes even after ascending from the ritual bath; <b>but if he did</b> intend to <b>assume a presumptive status</b> of ritual purity for a minor matter, <b>he may not</b> intend to <b>assume a presumptive status</b> of ritual purity for a major matter, as his intention was fixed when he ascended from the ritual bath.
In relation to the above, the Gemara explains: <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught</b> that one <b>whose foot is still in the water</b> is considered to be still immersing himself? <b>Rabbi Pedat said: It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Mikvaot</i> 7:6): In the case of <b>a ritual bath that was measured and</b> found to <b>contain exactly forty <i>se’a</i></b> of water, <b>and</b> then <b>two</b> individuals <b>descended and immersed one after the other, the first one is ritually pure,</b> since he immersed in a valid ritual bath, <b>but the second is ritually impure.</b> Because a small amount of water clings to the first individual, the ritual bath subsequently holds less than the requisite amount. Therefore, it does not purify the second individual. <b>Rabbi Yehuda said: If the feet of the first one were</b> still <b>touching the water,</b> so that he had not yet exited the ritual bath entirely while the second one immersed, <b>the second is also ritually pure.</b> This teaches that Rabbi Yehuda is of the opinion that one who still has a foot in the water is considered to be inside the ritual bath.
<b>Rav Naḥman said</b> that <b>Rabba bar Avuh said:</b> This <b>dispute</b> between the Rabbis and Rabbi Yehuda pertains <b>to higher standards of ritual purity</b> established <b>by the Sages,</b> where the obligation to immerse is due to a rabbinical ordinance. <b>However,</b> when the purpose of the immersion is to transition <b>from</b> full-fledged <b>ritual impurity to purity, everyone agrees</b> that <b>the second</b> individual <b>is impure. And this is</b> in accordance with the statement <b>of Rabbi Pedat</b> on this topic, who asserts that only Rabbi Yehuda maintains that one may assume a presumptive ritually pure status if one of his feet is still in the ritual bath.
<b>There are</b> those <b>who say</b> a different version of this statement: <b>Rav Naḥman said</b> that <b>Rabba bar Avuh said:</b> This <b>dispute</b> is referring only to one who is obligated to immerse in order to transition <b>from ritual impurity to purity, but with regard to higher standards of ritual purity</b> established <b>by the Sages, everyone agrees</b> that <b>even the second</b> individual <b>is ritually pure</b> if the foot of the first is still in contact with the water. <b>And</b> consequently, this opinion <b>disagrees</b> with the statement <b>of Rabbi Pedat,</b> since, according to this version, all agree that with regard to higher standards of ritual purity, the immersion continues as long as a single foot remains in the water.
With regard to the discussion above, <b>Ulla said: I asked Rabbi Yoḥanan: According to Rabbi Yehuda, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>immersing</b> small vessels, such as <b>needles and knitting needles, on the head of the first</b> individual immersing himself? Since Rabbi Yehuda maintains that one whose foot is still in the water is considered as immersed in the ritual bath, does this mean that his body and even his wet hair can serve as part of the ritual bath?
The Gemara clarifies the dilemma raised by this question: Does <b>Rabbi Yehuda accept</b> only the principle of <b>lowering the partition,</b> meaning that an item positioned above another item is considered as if it continued downward, and therefore the water on the body of the one immersing is viewed as descending into the ritual bath, so that the bath retains its requisite size; but <b>he does not accept</b> the concept of <b>raising the partition,</b> so that the water in the ritual bath is not considered to rise up to one’s head, making him part of the ritual bath as well? <b>Or, perhaps he also accepts</b> the principle of <b>raising the partition,</b> which means that one is indeed considered part of the ritual bath?
Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to him: You</b> already <b>learned</b> it in the <i>Tosefta</i> (<i>Mikvaot</i> 3:3): If there are <b>three depressions in</b> the bed of <b>a stream that</b> are not completely dry, <b>an upper one, a lower one, and a middle one;</b> and <b>the upper and lower ones</b> hold <b>twenty <i>se’a</i> each, while the middle one</b> contains <b>forty <i>se’a</i>, and a flow of rain runs between them,</b> thereby linking the depressions one to another, <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> My colleague, Rabbi <b>Meir, would say:</b> One may <b>immerse in the upper one.</b> This indicates that Rabbi Meir holds that the waters of the middle depression, which contains the requisite amount of water for a ritual bath, are considered to have ascended by means of the rainwater into the higher depression. Since Rabbi Yehuda cites his colleague’s statement without comment, he evidently accepts the principle of raising the partition.
Ulla raised a difficulty: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b>

Daf 19b

Rabbi <b>Meir would say:</b> One may <b>immerse in the upper one, and I say: In the lower one but not in the upper one.</b> This demonstrates that Rabbi Yehuda does not accept the principle of raising the partition. Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to him: If this</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>is taught, it is taught,</b> and I cannot take issue with it. Therefore, it is clear that the matter is held in dispute between <i>tanna’im</i> and that Rabbi Yehuda does not accept the principle of raising the partition.
§ It is taught in the mishna: <b>One who immersed for</b> the purpose of eating <b>non-sacred</b> food with the intention of <b>assuming a presumptive status</b> of ritual purity <b>for non-sacred</b> food <b>it is prohibited for him</b> to eat <b>tithes.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>Whose</b> opinion is expressed in <b>the mishna? It is</b> that of <b>the Rabbis, who differentiate between non-sacred</b> produce <b>and tithes,</b> since they maintain the following: If one who is required by rabbinic law to immerse touches non-sacred food, it remains pure, but if he comes into contact with tithes they are rendered ritually impure.
However, in that case, <b>say the latter clause</b> of the mishna, which states that <b>the garments of an <i>am ha’aretz</i>,</b> who is not careful with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual purity, <b>are</b> considered to be rendered ritually impure by the impurity imparted by the <b>treading</b> of a <i>zav</i>, which is considered a primary source of ritual impurity, <b>for <i>perushin</i>,</b> individuals who are careful to eat even non-sacred food in a state of purity. <b>The garments of <i>perushin</i>,</b> although they are careful to remain ritually pure, are nevertheless considered to be rendered impure by the <b>treading</b> of a <i>zav</i> <b>for</b> priests who partake of <b><i>teruma</i>.</b> Consequently, the latter clause differs from the opinion of the Rabbis in the earlier clause.
Therefore, <b>we have arrived at</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir, who said: Non-sacred</b> produce <b>and tithes are similar to one another,</b> as this clause of the mishna does not distinguish between those eating non-sacred food and those eating tithes. Is <b>the earlier clause</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis and the latter clause</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Yes;</b> although it is unusual, in this instance we must explain that <b>the earlier clause</b> was said by <b>the Rabbis and the latter clause</b> by <b>Rabbi Meir.</b>
However, <b>Rav Aḥa bar Adda would teach</b> that <b>five levels</b> of ritual purity are listed <b>in the latter clause</b> of the mishna, by counting the clause that states that the clothes of those who eat non-sacred produce in a state of purity are ritually impure for tithes, <b>and</b> in this way <b>he establishes</b> the <b>entire</b> mishna <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b>
§ <b>Rav Mari said:</b> They <b>learn from</b> the mishna that <b>non-sacred</b> produce <b>that was prepared according to</b> the level of <b>ritual purity</b> required <b>for sacrificial</b> food, i.e., with the same stringencies as required for sacrificial food, is <b>like sacrificial</b> food. <b>From where</b> is this deduced?

Daf 20a

It is deduced <b>from</b> the fact <b>that it is not taught in their regard</b> that those who eat non-sacred produce according to the level of ritual purity required for sacrificial food must treat the produce with <b>a higher standard</b> with regard to their degree of purity, like those who actually partake of sacrificial food.
The Gemara asks: <b>But perhaps</b> the reason for <b>this</b> fact, <b>that a higher standard is not taught with regard to those</b> who actually partake of sacrificial food, is that these foods are not on a distinct level of ritual purity, <b>as, if they are similar to</b> the level of <b><i>teruma</i>, <i>teruma</i> has</b> already been <b>taught; and if they are similar to non-sacred</b> produce, <b>non-sacred produce has</b> also already been <b>taught. As we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that they are not considered to be on a level of their own: <b>Non-sacred</b> foods <b>prepared according to the</b> level of ritual <b>purity</b> required <b>for sacrificial</b> food <b>are like non-sacred</b> food; <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Tzadok, says: They are like <i>teruma</i>,</b> but not like sacrificial food. Therefore, the fact that this level is not explicitly mentioned affords no proof.
<b>Rather,</b> the proof is derived from <b>the last clause</b> in the mishna: <b>Yosei ben Yo’ezer was the most pious</b> member <b>of the priesthood, and</b> yet <b>his cloth was</b> considered impure by the <b>treading</b> of a <i>zav</i> <b>for</b> those who ate <b>sacrificial</b> food. <b>Yoḥanan ben Gudgeda would eat</b> non-sacred foods prepared <b>according to the</b> level of ritual <b>purity</b> required <b>for sacrificial</b> food <b>all his days, and</b> nevertheless <b>his cloth was</b> considered rendered impure by the <b>treading</b> of a <i>zav</i> <b>for</b> those preparing the <b>purification</b> waters.
The Gemara infers from this: <b>For</b> the <b>purifying</b> waters, <b>yes,</b> his cloth was considered to have ritual impurity imparted by treading, but <b>for sacrificial</b> food, <b>no,</b> it was not considered to have ritual impurity imparted by treading. <b>Apparently, he maintains</b> that <b>non-sacred</b> produce <b>prepared according to</b> the level of ritual <b>purity</b> required <b>for sacrificial</b> food is <b>like sacrificial</b> food, as one who is particular to preserve the ritual purity required for sacrificial food even with regard to non-sacred produce is considered pure even with regard to sacrificial food themselves.
§ With regard to the particular care that must be taken to prevent any suspicion that one’s clothes have contracted impurity, <b>Rabbi Yonatan ben Elazar said:</b> If <b>the shawl of</b> one who was stringent with regard to ritual purity <b>fell off of him,</b> and <b>he said to another</b> person: <b>Give it to me, and he gave it to him,</b> the shawl is <b>impure.</b> Even if the other individual is himself pure, since his attention was diverted at that moment from being cautious with regard to impurity, it is as though the shawl were rendered impure. Similarly, <b>Rabbi Yonatan ben Amram says:</b> If <b>one’s Shabbat clothes were switched for his weekday clothes and he wore them, they are impure.</b> His assumption that they were different clothes than the clothes he had intended to wear is enough of a distraction to spoil his caution against impurity.
<b>Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok said:</b> There was <b>an incident involving two women who were wives of <i>ḥaverim</i>,</b> who are meticulous in observance of <i>halakha</i> especially with regard to matters of impurity, <b>whose clothes were switched in the bathhouse; and the incident came before Rabbi Akiva and he declared</b> the clothes <b>impure.</b> This demonstrates that an unintentional act is considered a lapse of attention, which renders the items impure, even if there was no other reason to consider them impure.
<b>Rabbi Oshaya strongly objects to this: However, if that is so,</b> one who <b>inserted his hand into a basket to take a loaf of wheat</b> bread, <b>and a loaf of barley bread came up in his hand</b> instead; <b>in that case, too,</b> will you say that the loaf <b>is rendered impure?</b>
<b>And if you would say, indeed,</b> this is correct, <b>but isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who is minding a barrel</b> to ensure its ritual purity <b>on the assumption</b> that it is a barrel <b>of wine and</b> it <b>is found</b> to be <b>of oil, it is ritually pure</b> in the sense that it does not <b>transmit impurity?</b> This indicates that one’s lack of knowledge with regard to the identity of the item he is minding does not itself cause impurity. The Gemara rejects this: <b>But according to your</b> line of <b>reasoning, say the latter clause</b> of that same <i>baraita</i>: <b>And it is prohibited to be eaten,</b> which indicates that the supervision is insufficient in this case. The Gemara asks: <b>Why</b> is it that the barrel’s status is pure and yet there is a prohibition against eating its contents? If the supervisor’s error does not harm the food’s ritually pure status, one should likewise be permitted to eat it.
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya said:</b> The <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to one who says: I guarded it from things that render it impure but not from things that invalidate it.</b> In other words, he was careful to guard it only from being rendered impure with a severe degree of impurity, which causes anything it renders impure to render others impure in turn, but not from a lesser degree of impurity that merely invalidates it for use but does not enable it to render other items ritually impure. Since he guarded it from impurity, it is considered pure with regard to rendering other items impure, but it still may not be eaten, in case it was invalidated by an impure object.
The Gemara asks: <b>And is there guarding by half</b> measures; can it be said that one was careful with regard to only a particular type of impurity? The Gemara responds: <b>Yes, as it is</b> indeed <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>If one inserted his hand into a basket</b> filled with figs, <b>and the basket</b> was placed <b>on his shoulder, and a shovel</b> was <b>in the basket, and his mind was on the basket</b> to guard it from impurity, <b>but his mind was not on the shovel, the basket is pure and the shovel is impure.</b>
The Gemara asks: Why is <b>the basket pure? Let the shovel render the basket ritually impure,</b> if the former is in fact impure. The Gemara answers: The <i>halakha</i> is that <b>a vessel cannot render</b> a different <b>vessel impure.</b> Therefore, the basket remains pure. The Gemara asks another question: <b>And let it render impure</b> any food <b>that is inside the basket,</b> as food is not a vessel and can therefore be rendered impure by a vessel. <b>Ravina said:</b> The <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to one who says: I guarded</b> the shovel <b>from things that render it ritually impure,</b> which is why it cannot render other objects impure, <b>but</b> I did <b>not</b> guard it <b>from things that invalidate it,</b> so it is impure. Consequently, there is no proof from here that the contents of the barrel in the earlier case may not be eaten.
Returning to the prior discussion, the Gemara states that <b>in any case</b> it is <b>difficult.</b> Why should an object be impure just because the one guarding it was mistaken with regard to the identity of its contents; how would this accord with the <i>baraita</i> that explicitly taught that if one minds a barrel under the assumption that it is wine and it turns out to contain oil, the oil is pure and cannot render others impure?
<b>And Rabba bar Avuh raised a further objection:</b> There was <b>an incident involving a certain woman who came before Rabbi Yishmael and said to him: Rabbi, I wove this garment in a ritually pure state, but my mind was not on it to guard its</b> state of <b>purity.</b> In other words, although I did not intend to guard it in this manner, I am certain that no impurity came into contact with it. <b>And during the interrogations that Rabbi Yishmael conducted with her,</b> to see if it had remained in a state of purity, <b>she</b> happened to <b>say to him: Rabbi, a menstruating woman pulled the rope with me</b> as I was weaving, and the garment was therefore rendered fully impure by a menstruating woman moving it. <b>Rabbi Yishmael said: How great are the words of the Sages when they said:</b> If <b>one’s mind</b> is focused <b>on guarding it, it is pure; if one’s mind is not</b> focused <b>on guarding it, it is impure.</b> Since she was not focused on preserving the garment’s pure state, it contracted impurity without her noticing.
There was <b>another incident involving a certain woman who came before Rabbi Yishmael</b> and <b>said to him: Rabbi, I wove this cloth in</b> a state of ritual <b>purity, but my mind was not on it to guard it</b> from impurity. <b>And during the interrogations that Rabbi Yishmael conducted with her, she said to him: Rabbi, a thread</b> of <b>mine</b> that was woven into the cloth <b>snapped and I tied it with my mouth.</b> It can be assumed that the thread became moist from her spittle, which means that if that thread was touched by a source of impurity, the cloth would be rendered ritually impure by contact with impure liquids. This is because the Sages decreed that any impurity that touches liquid renders the liquid ritually impure to the first degree, so any vessel that comes into contact with the liquid would be impure to the second degree.
<b>Rabbi Yishmael said: How great are the words of the Sages when they said:</b> If <b>one’s mind is</b> focused <b>on guarding it, it is pure; if one’s mind is not</b> focused <b>on guarding it, it is impure.</b> In any case, if one intends to keep something in a state of ritual purity, a mistake on his part with regard to its identity is not considered enough of a distraction to render the item impure, unlike the <i>baraita</i> that explicitly taught that if one minds a barrel under the assumption that it is wine and it turns out to contain oil, the oil is pure and cannot render others impure.
The Gemara clarifies: <b>Granted, according to Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok</b> there is no difficulty, as in the case of the two wives of <i>ḥaverim</i> whose garments were switched it can be said that <b>each of them says</b> to herself: <b>My colleague is the wife of an <i>am ha’aretz</i>,</b> and not a <i>ḥaver</i>. <b>And she diverts her mind from</b> her garments, as she is certain that they have already been rendered impure, and a distraction of this kind makes it likely that the garment contracted impurity. It is therefore considered impure.
Likewise, <b>according to Rabbi Yonatan ben Amram</b> there is no difficulty either, as with regard to the case of one who switched his Shabbat clothes with his weekday clothes, it can <b>also</b> be said that <b>since he is more protective of Shabbat clothes, he will divert his mind from</b> that higher level of protection if he thinks that they are weekday garments. A distraction of this kind makes it likely that the garment contracted impurity, so it is considered impure. <b>But according to Rabbi Yonatan ben Elazar,</b> who deals with the case where one’s shawl fell and another person lifts it up, why should this be considered a distraction? <b>Let him guard</b> his garments from ritual impurity while they are <b>in the other person’s hands;</b> why should they be considered impure?
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> It is <b>a presumption</b> that <b>a person does not guard that which is in another’s hand.</b> Since the object is in the hands of another, he will inevitably be distracted from guarding it. The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> can one indeed <b>not</b> guard an item in the hand of another?

Daf 20b

<b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>one whose donkey drivers and workers,</b> who were <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, <b>were bearing pure</b> food, without touching the pure food itself but only the earthenware vessels containing them, even if <b>he distanced himself from them</b> as they walked by <b>more than a <i>mil</i>, his pure</b> foods <b>are pure.</b> Since the workers are unaware of his departure, he is still considered to be guarding the food in their possession and need not be concerned that they may have touched the pure foods. <b>But if he said to them: Go, and I will follow behind you,</b> then <b>once they are no longer within his eyesight, his pure</b> foods <b>are impure.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is different</b> in <b>the first clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i>, where the food remains pure, <b>and what is different</b> in <b>the latter clause,</b> where the food is impure? <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak Nappaḥa said: The first clause</b> is referring <b>to one who purifies his donkey drivers and workers for this</b> purpose, meaning that he ensured that they immersed and purified themselves beforehand, so that concern for impurity was removed.
The Gemara questions this: <b>If so,</b> in <b>the latter clause</b> they should <b>also</b> be pure. The Gemara responds: <b>An <i>am ha’aretz</i> is not particular about the contact of his colleague,</b> and therefore there is concern that they might have encountered another <i>am ha’aretz</i> on the way, who touched the produce and thereby rendered it impure.
The Gemara counters: <b>If so,</b> in <b>the first</b> clause of the <i>baraita</i> there should <b>also</b> be concern that they might have met an <i>am ha’aretz</i>, and despite the employer’s warning to his workers to stay ritually pure, they are not careful with regard to the impurity of another <i>am ha’aretz</i>. The Gemara answers: The first clause is referring to a situation <b>when he comes across them</b> via <b>a circuitous path.</b> Since he is not walking directly behind them but can appear from the sides, they cannot always see him. Consequently, they are concerned that he may return at any moment. Therefore, they are careful not to render themselves ritually impure, and they are also wary of the contact of other <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, although they are not usually particular about the contact of their colleagues.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>If so,</b> in <b>the latter clause, too,</b> since he can arrive from around a corner at any given moment, they should certainly be cautious. The Gemara responds: <b>Since he said to them: Go and I will follow behind you, they rely</b> on this, and they do not consider themselves to be under observation. Consequently, they are not particular about the contact of another <i>am ha’aretz</i>.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> Concerning several matters there is greater <b>stringency with regard to sacrificial</b> food <b>than with regard to <i>teruma</i>,</b> a portion of the produce designated for the priest. This expresses itself in many ways, the first being <b>that one may immerse vessels inside</b> other <b>vessels</b> to purify them <b>for <i>teruma</i>; but not for sacrificial</b> food, for which one must immerse each vessel separately. Another difference is that the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>the back</b> of a vessel <b>and</b> its <b>inside and</b> its <b>place for gripping</b> apply <b>to</b> vessels used for <b><i>teruma</i>,</b> meaning that each part of the vessel has its own use and is considered a separate vessel in that it does not convey impurity to the other parts of the vessel when it contracts impurity; <b>but not to sacrificial</b> food, for which an impure section of the vessel does convey impurity to all the other sections.
Likewise, <b>one who carries an object trodden</b> on by a <i>zav</i>, a man suffering from gonorrhea, <b>may carry <i>teruma</i></b> at the same time, if he is careful that neither he nor the impure object should come into contact with the <i>teruma</i>, <b>but</b> this may <b>not</b> be done with <b>sacrificial</b> food. <b>The garments of those who eat <i>teruma</i></b> are like an object trodden on by a <i>zav</i> <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> food.
The mishna lists other stringencies that apply to sacrificial foods but not to <i>teruma</i>: <b>The characteristics of <i>teruma</i> are not like the characteristics of sacrificial</b> food, <b>as in</b> the case of vessels that are used with <b>sacrificial</b> food, if one has a garment or vessel that is tied up <b>he must untie</b> it <b>and dry</b> it if there was any moisture on it, as both a knot and absorbed moisture are considered interpositions that prevent the water of the ritual bath from reaching the entire garment. <b>And he may</b> then <b>immerse</b> them, <b>and afterward he may tie</b> them up again if he wishes. <b>But with regard to <i>teruma</i> he may,</b> if he so desires, <b>tie</b> up the garment <b>and then immerse</b> it without any concern that the knot might be considered an interposition.
<b>Vessels</b> that were fashioned and <b>completed in purity</b> nevertheless <b>require immersion</b> to be considered pure <b>for sacrificial</b> food<b>s, but not for <i>teruma</i>. A vessel combines</b> all the food <b>that is in it</b> with regard <b>to sacrificial</b> food, meaning that if one piece of food becomes impure all the other pieces become impure as well; <b>but not</b> with regard <b>to <i>teruma</i>,</b> concerning which each piece is treated independently.
The mishna continues the list of differences between sacrificial food and <i>teruma</i>. <b>Sacrificial</b> food <b>that is impure with fourth-degree</b> impurity <b>is disqualified,</b> meaning that the sacrificial food is rendered impure but it does not impart impurity to other items. <b><i>Teruma</i> is disqualified when it is impure with third-degree</b> impurity; it is not susceptible to fourth-degree impurity at all. <b>And with regard to <i>teruma</i>, if one of one’s hands became impure</b> by rabbinic law that renders only the hands impure, <b>its counterpart,</b> i.e., the other hand, remains <b>pure. But with regard to sacrificial</b> food, if one hand becomes impure <b>he must immerse them both, as one hand renders its counterpart impure with regard to sacrificial</b> food <b>but not with regard to <i>teruma</i>. One may eat dry foods,</b> i.e., foods that have never come into contact with liquid and are therefore not susceptible to impurity, <b>with impure hands when</b> it is <b><i>teruma</i>, but not</b> when it is <b>sacrificial</b> food.

Daf 21a

<b>An acute mourner [<i>onen</i>],</b> i.e., someone who has experienced the loss of a close relative on that day, who had not come into contact with the deceased; <b>and one who is lacking atonement,</b> i.e., someone who still needs to bring an offering to complete his purification procedure, such as a <i>zav</i> or a woman after childbirth, both <b>require immersion</b> in order <b>to</b> eat <b>sacrificial</b> food. The <i>onen</i> would immerse after the day has passed and the one lacking atonement would immerse after the requisite offering is brought. <b>However,</b> immersion in these cases is <b>not</b> necessary <b>for</b> eating <b><i>teruma</i>.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> It is taught in the mishna that one may not immerse one vessel inside another if they will be used for sacrificial food, though this may be done for <i>teruma</i>. The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to sacrificial</b> food, <b>what is the reason</b> that one may <b>not</b> immerse vessels in this manner? <b>Rabbi Ila said: Because the weight of the</b> inner <b>vessel causes an interposition</b> between the water and the vessels. That is, the innermost vessel weighs down on the bottom one, not allowing the water to reach the two vessels’ point of contact.
The Gemara poses a question on this explanation: <b>But is it not so that since the latter clause</b> of the mishna mentions that sacrificial foods and <i>teruma</i> differ <b>with regard to</b> matters of <b>interposition, the first clause</b> of the mishna must <b>not</b> be <b>with regard to</b> matters of <b>interposition,</b> but is referring to a different consideration? <b>As it teaches in the latter part</b> of the mishna: <b>The characteristics of <i>teruma</i> are not like the characteristics of sacrificial</b> food, <b>as in</b> the case of <b>sacrificial</b> food, if one has a garment or vessel that is tied up <b>he must untie</b> it <b>and dry</b> it, <b>and he may</b> then <b>immerse</b> them, <b>and afterward he may tie</b> them up again if he wishes; <b>but with regard to <i>teruma</i> he may tie</b> up the garment <b>and then immerse</b> it. This latter clause shows that there the Sages were concerned for any possibility of interposition with regard to sacrificial food, so most likely the earlier clause of the mishna is due to a different reason.
The Gemara answers: No, this is not necessarily so. In both <b>the first clause and the latter clause</b> of the mishna the concern is <b>due to interposition, and</b> it is nevertheless <b>necessary</b> to teach us both cases. <b>For had</b> the mishna <b>taught us</b> only <b>the first clause,</b> i.e., that one may not immerse one vessel within another, <b>I would have said</b> that <b>this is the reason</b> one may <b>not</b> do so <b>for sacrificial</b> food: <b>Because there is the weight of</b> the interior <b>vessel</b> that must be taken into account. <b>But</b> in <b>the latter clause, where there is no weight of a vessel</b> to be considered, <b>I will say</b> that <b>it is not</b> considered an interposition <b>for sacrificial</b> food <b>either. And had it taught us</b> only <b>the latter clause</b> dealing with the knotted garments, <b>I would have said</b> that <b>this is the reason one may not</b> do so <b>for sacrificial</b> food: <b>Because</b> it is possible

Daf 21b

<b>that</b> it is the nature of <b>knots</b> to <b>tighten</b> even more <b>in water,</b> creating an interposition that bars the water from entering all the way, <b>but</b> in the case of <b>the first clause</b> of the mishna, which deals with one vessel inside another and <b>where water</b> by nature <b>causes the</b> top <b>vessel to</b> lighten and <b>float</b> away from the lower vessel rather than weigh down on it, I would have said that <b>it is not</b> considered an <b>interposition.</b> It is therefore <b>necessary</b> for the <i>halakha</i> to be stated in both cases.
The Gemara comments: <b>Rabbi Ila</b> here conforms <b>to his</b> standard line of <b>reasoning</b> in considering these two issues as one, <b>as Rabbi Ila said</b> that <b>Rabbi Ḥanina bar Pappa said: They taught ten stringencies</b> of sacrificial food <b>here</b> in this mishna, rather than the apparent eleven. The <b>first five</b> stringencies apply <b>both to the sacrificial</b> foods themselves and <b>to non-sacred</b> food <b>that was prepared according to</b> the standards of <b>purity of sacrificial</b> food, whereas the <b>last</b> five apply only <b>to</b> actual <b>sacrificial</b> food <b>but not to non-sacred</b> food <b>that was prepared according to</b> the standards of <b>purity of sacrificial</b> food. The fact that Rabbi Ila counts only ten cases in the mishna shows that he considered the two cases discussed above to be of the same category, and therefore they are counted together as one stringency.
The Gemara explains Rabbi Ila’s statement. <b>What is the reason</b> for this distinction? With regard to the <b>first five</b> stringencies, <b>which have a connection to impurity</b> as defined <b>by Torah law</b> because ignoring them can lead to a case of impurity by Torah law as opposed to merely rabbinic law, <b>the Sages decreed these</b> stringencies <b>both for</b> actual <b>sacrificial</b> food <b>and for non-sacred</b> food <b>prepared according to</b> the standards of <b>purity of sacrificial</b> food. However, with regard to the <b>last</b> five, <b>which do not have a connection to impurity by Torah law,</b> as their entire impurity is based on a rabbinic decree, <b>the Sages decreed these</b> stringencies only <b>for</b> actual <b>sacrificial</b> food. But <b>with regard to non-sacred</b> food <b>made according to</b> the standards of <b>purity of sacrificial</b> food, <b>the Sages did not decree</b> these stringencies <b>for</b> such foods.
<b>Rava</b> disagreed with Rabbi Ila. He <b>said</b> that <b>since</b> the reason for the stringency in <b>the latter clause is due to</b> concern for interposition, this implies that the reason for the stringency in <b>the first clause is not due to interposition,</b> but to a different reason. <b>And</b> with regard to the stringency in <b>the first clause</b> that one may not immerse one vessel within another, <b>this is the reasoning:</b> It is <b>a</b> rabbinic <b>decree</b> to ensure <b>that one not immerse</b> small vessels, such as <b>needles and hooks, inside a vessel whose mouth</b> is less than the width of <b>the tube of a wineskin.</b> In such a case the water in the bottle would not be considered attached to the rest of the ritual bath, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Mikvaot</i> 6:7): <b>The joining of</b> different bodies of water in cases of <b>ritual baths</b> takes place if the opening between the two bodies is at least as wide <b>as</b> the width of <b>the tube of a wineskin,</b> counting both the <b>thickness</b> of the wall of the tube

Daf 22a

<b>and its space, which is equivalent to</b> the width of <b>two fingers going around in their place,</b> i.e., a space large enough to insert two fingers and twist them around inside. If one body of water contains the requisite forty <i>se’a</i>, while another, adjacent body is lacking this amount, then if the opening between the two bodies of water is wider than this measurement, the two bodies are considered as one, and the smaller body is also considered an acceptable ritual bath. Since any opening smaller than this is not considered to connect two bodies of water, the water inside a bottle with a narrow mouth would be considered disconnected from the water of the ritual bath, and smaller vessels inside such a narrow-necked vessel would not be considered as having come into contact with the water of the ritual bath. The Sages therefore enacted a decree rendering prohibited the immersion of any vessel inside another vessel.
The Gemara notes: Rava <b>holds in accordance with this</b> statement <b>that Rav Naḥman said</b> that <b>Rabba bar Avuh said: They taught eleven stringencies</b> of sacrificial food <b>here</b> in this mishna, rather than Rabbi Ila’s ten. The <b>first six</b> stringencies apply <b>both to sacrificial</b> food itself and <b>to non-sacred</b> food <b>that was prepared according to</b> the standards of <b>purity of sacrificial</b> food, whereas the <b>last</b> five apply only <b>to</b> actual <b>sacrificial</b> food <b>but not to non-sacred</b> food <b>that was prepared according to</b> the standards of <b>purity of sacrificial</b> food.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> practical difference <b>is there between</b> the opinion <b>of Rava,</b> i.e., that the Sages rendered it prohibited to immerse one vessel inside of another because they were concerned lest one immerse needles in a vessel whose mouth is narrower than the tube of a wineskin, <b>and</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Ila,</b> who holds that their concern was about interposition?
The Gemara answers: <b>The</b> practical difference <b>between them</b> is in the case of <b>a basket or</b> a wicker <b>bin [<i>gargutni</i>] that one has filled</b> with smaller <b>vessels and</b> has <b>immersed them</b> all together. <b>According to</b> Rabbi Ila, <b>the one who said</b> it is prohibited to immerse one vessel inside another <b>due to</b> the concern of <b>interposition,</b> in this case as well <b>there is</b> such a concern, as the inner vessels might weigh down against the basket and prevent the water from touching the contact points. But <b>according to</b> Rava, <b>the one who said</b> it is prohibited <b>due to</b> a rabbinic <b>decree lest one immerse needles and hooks inside a vessel whose mouth does not have</b> the width of <b>the tube of a wineskin, there are no</b> such things as <b>baskets and</b> wicker <b>bins whose mouth does not have</b> the width of <b>the tube of a wineskin,</b> and therefore the decree would not apply to them.
The Gemara notes: <b>And Rava follows his</b> line of <b>reasoning, as Rava said: A basket or</b> wicker <b>bin that one has filled</b> with smaller <b>vessels and</b> has <b>immersed them</b> all together, <b>they are pure</b> in all regards, even for sacrificial food.
Rava stated a second teaching with regard to baskets and bins as well: <b>And</b> in the case of <b>a ritual bath that one divided</b> into two sections <b>by</b> inserting <b>a basket or</b> wicker <b>bin,</b> so that each section is left with less than the required forty <i>se’a</i>, if <b>one immerses there, his immersion is ineffective for him.</b> Despite the certainty that water seeps through the basket or bin, this is not enough to join the two incomplete sections of the ritual bath to be counted as one. We know that this is so, <b>for the earth is entirely porous, and</b> nevertheless <b>we</b> do not rely on this to allow several adjacent, small ditches full of water to add up to forty <i>se’a</i>, but rather <b>require that there be forty <i>se’a</i></b> together <b>in one place.</b>
The Gemara comments: <b>And this</b> <i>halakha</i>, that small vessels such as needles cannot be immersed inside a vessel with a narrow opening, <b>applies</b> only if he immersed them <b>in a pure vessel,</b> which does not require purification on its own. <b>But</b> if he did so <b>in an impure vessel,</b> which requires purification in its own right, <b>since the immersion is effective for the whole of the</b> outer <b>vessel,</b> including its inside, <b>it is also effective for</b> the <b>vessels that are inside of it.</b> Since the water that enters the outer vessel is considered attached to the rest of the ritual bath for purposes of purification of the outer vessel, so is it considered attached with regard to the purification of the inner vessels.
We know this, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Mikvaot</i> 6:2): With regard to <b>vessels that one filled with</b> other <b>vessels and immersed them</b> all together, <b>they are pure,</b> regardless of the width of the opening of the outer vessel. <b>And if he did not immerse, the joining of waters</b> is not effective <b>until they are joined like the</b> width of the <b>tube of a wineskin.</b> This second sentence of the mishna is unclear, and the Gemara seeks to clarify it. <b>What is</b> the mishna <b>saying</b> here when it says: <b>And if he did not immerse?</b> The Gemara explains. <b>This is what</b> the mishna <b>is saying: And if he has no need to immerse</b> the outer vessel, as it was already pure, <b>and</b> similarly in a case of two bodies of <b>water</b> that are <b>joined</b> together by means of a hole, it is not valid <b>until</b> the water <b>is joined</b> through a space as wide <b>as the tube of a wineskin.</b>
§ The Gemara notes: <b>And this</b> dispute between <b>Rava and Rabbi Ila is</b> also a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>a basket or a</b> wicker <b>bin that one filled</b> with <b>vessels and</b> then <b>immersed them, whether for</b> purposes of <b>sacrificial</b> food <b>or for</b> purposes of <b><i>teruma</i>, they are pure.</b> This is identical to Rava’s opinion. <b>Abba Shaul says:</b> They are pure <b>for</b> purposes of <b><i>teruma</i> but not for</b> purposes of <b>sacrificial</b> food. This is identical to Rabbi Ila’s opinion.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> in light of these two reasons we have given for concern with regard to immersing vessels inside other vessels, this should <b>not</b> be permitted for <b><i>teruma</i> either.</b> The Gemara responds: <b>For whom do we say</b> the principle that one vessel may not be immersed inside another? For <b><i>ḥaverim</i>,</b> individuals devoted to the meticulous observance of mitzvot, especially <i>halakhot</i> of ritual purity. Others do not carefully follow these <i>halakhot</i> in any event. And <b><i>ḥaverim</i> know</b> such things <b>well,</b> that water is considered detached from the ritual bath if it is separated by a narrow opening, and that if one vessel is weighing down on another, preventing the water from reaching that spot, the top vessel must be lifted to allow the water to touch all parts of the vessel. Therefore, there is no need to apply these concerns and stringencies to the case of <i>teruma</i>.
The Gemara counters with another question. <b>If so,</b> we should say the same thing in the case of <b>sacrificial</b> food <b>too,</b> i.e., that all these <i>halakhot</i> are for <i>ḥaverim</i>, who meticulously follow ritual purity for sacrificial food and inquire about such <i>halakhot</i>. Why, then, did the Sages apply these concerns and stringencies to the case of sacrificial food? The Gemara responds: With regard to sacrificial food they were concerned that a <b>common person</b> [<b><i>am ha’aretz</i></b>], who is not meticulous about ritual purity, may <b>see</b> the <i>ḥaver</i> immersing small vessels inside of large vessels, <b>and</b> will then <b>go and immerse</b> vessels of his own in this manner. But he will not take the same precautions as the <i>ḥaver</i> would, ensuring that the outer vessel has a wide opening and that the vessels on top do not weigh down on the lower ones.
The Gemara objects: But the same concern could be raised with regard to <b><i>teruma</i> as well.</b> It is possible that <b>an <i>am ha’aretz</i></b> may <b>see</b> the <i>ḥaver</i> immerse vessels for <i>teruma</i> in this manner, <b>and</b> he will then <b>go and immerse</b> his vessels this way, without taking the precautions that the <i>ḥaver</i> would take. The Gemara answers: <b>We do not accept</b> <i>teruma</i> <b>from</b> <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, as they are not trustworthy with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual purity, and therefore it does not matter if the vessels he uses for <i>teruma</i> are not immersed properly. Therefore, the Sages were not concerned that the <i>am ha’aretz</i> may come to a misunderstanding when observing a <i>ḥaver</i> immersing vessels within vessels.
The Gemara continues its line of questioning. If so, <b>we should likewise not accept sacrificial</b> food <b>from</b> <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, since they are not sufficiently meticulous with ritual purity, and we should therefore not care if they immerse their vessels improperly. The Gemara responds: The <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>will have</b> feelings of <b>antagonism</b> if sacrificial food is not accepted from him, and this would lead to internal discord and conflict within Israel.
The Gemara asks: If so, in the case of <b><i>teruma</i> he will also have</b> feelings of <b>antagonism</b> if <i>teruma</i> is not accepted from him. Why were the Sages concerned about this factor only with regard to sacrificial food and not <i>teruma</i>? The Gemara replies: An <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>does not care</b> if his <i>teruma</i> is not accepted by <i>ḥaverim</i>, <b>as</b> he can always <b>go</b> and <b>give</b> his <i>teruma</i> <b>to an <i>am ha’aretz</i> priest</b> who is <b>his friend</b> and who will accept it from him. In the case of sacrificial food, however, there is only one Temple, and care must be taken not to make the <i>amei ha’aretz</i> feel they are being rejected.
The Gemara notes that this sensitivity of not causing offense to the <i>am ha’aretz</i> is expressed elsewhere as well: <b>And who is the <i>tanna</i> that is concerned for</b> such <b>antagonism</b> of <i>amei ha’aretz</i>? <b>It is Rabbi Yosei, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yosei said: For what</b> reason are <b>all</b> people, i.e., even <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, <b>trusted with regard to the purity of</b> their <b>wine and oil</b> that they bring to the Temple for sacrificial purposes <b>throughout the year?</b> Why is the status of these items not investigated to determine that they were prepared with the necessary regard for ritual purity? In order to avoid schisms among the people, <b>so that each and every individual should not go off and build a</b> private <b>altar for himself and burn a red heifer for himself.</b> Were the Sages to reject sacrificial wine and oil from <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, they would become alienated and go off and create schisms, going so far as to build their own separate temples and bring their own private offerings.
<b>Rav Pappa said: In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>do we accept testimony nowadays from an <i>am ha’aretz</i>,</b> despite the concern of some Sages that their carelessness with regard to observance of <i>halakha</i> might also lead to personal untrustworthiness? <b>In accordance with whom</b> is this done? <b>In accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei.</b>
§ We have established that the reason for leniency with regard to immersing one vessel inside another for <i>teruma</i> is based on the fact that we do not care if the vessels <i>amei ha’aretz</i> use for <i>teruma</i> are improperly purified, since we do not accept <i>teruma</i> from them in any event. The Gemara asks: <b>But let us be concerned about borrowing</b> vessels from them. Although <i>ḥaverim</i> do not accept <i>teruma</i> from an <i>am ha’aretz</i>, they do sometimes borrow their vessels and use them for <i>teruma</i>. It should therefore be a matter of concern for us if those vessels are not properly purified.
The Gemara proves that it is acceptable to borrow vessels from an <i>am ha’aretz</i>: <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Eduyyot</i> 1:14): <b>An earthenware vessel</b> of an <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>shields</b> all kinds of items from the ritual impurity imparted by a corpse. There are two applications of this fact: If there are objects or foods inside a tightly sealed earthenware vessel located inside a room containing a corpse, the vessel prevents the impurity from reaching the items inside it. Also, if there is a corpse in the first floor of a house and food or vessels are located in a second story of the same building, with an opening such as a skylight in the floor between the two stories, an earthenware vessel plugging up the opening will prevent the ritual impurity from spreading to the second story. This is <b>the statement of Beit Hillel. Beit Shammai say: It shields only food, drink, and earthenware vessels,</b> but not utensils of metal, wood, cloth, etc.
The mishna continues: <b>Beit Hillel said to Beit Shammai: For what</b> reason do you make this distinction? It is clear that a closed, pure earthenware vessel serves as a barrier, blocking the spread of impurity in a room with a corpse (see Numbers 19:15). <b>Beit Shammai said</b> to them: <b>Because</b> the earthenware vessel itself <b>is impure on account of</b> its contact with <b>an <i>am ha’aretz</i>.</b> The Sages decreed that anything touched by an <i>am ha’aretz</i> is impure, since such people are not meticulous or sufficiently knowledgeable about the <i>halakhot</i> of purity; therefore his vessels are considered impure. <b>And</b> the principle is that <b>an impure vessel does not serve as a barrier</b> from impurity. <b>Beit Hillel said to them: But didn’t you declare the food and drink inside</b> the earthenware vessel to be <b>pure?</b> If the vessel of an <i>am ha’aretz</i> cannot serve as a barrier, why do you say that some items inside it are pure? <b>Beit Shammai said to them: When we declared the food and drink inside it to be pure,</b>

Daf 22b

<b>we declared</b> them <b>pure for</b> the <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>himself,</b> not for <i>ḥaverim</i>, since <i>ḥaverim</i> in any event do not consider anything touched by an <i>am ha’aretz</i> to be pure. A <i>ḥaver</i> would therefore never relate to the food of an <i>am ha’aretz</i> as pure, and he would not use the earthenware vessels of an <i>am ha’aretz</i> for preparation of pure food, since an earthenware vessel cannot be purified through immersion. However, he might borrow a metal vessel, for instance, and purify it through immersion before using it for pure food.
<b>But</b> how <b>can we purify a vessel</b> of metal and other materials, <b>whose purity</b> would be relevant both <b>for you and for him?</b> A <i>ḥaver</i> may one day borrow a metal vessel from the <i>am ha’aretz</i>, and not realize that it was once inside an earthenware vessel in the same room as a corpse and as a result requires extensive purification from corpse contamination through the ashes of the red heifer, rather than mere immersion. Therefore, the <i>am ha’aretz</i> is informed that his vessels of metal and other materials have contracted impurity from the corpse, and must undergo the extensive purification process, and after this is done these vessels will now be fit for use of the <i>ḥaver</i> after mere immersion.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to this debate between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel: <b>Rabbi Yehoshua said: I am ashamed of your words, Beit Shammai,</b> for they are illogical. Is it <b>possible</b> that there should be a corpse on the first floor, with an earthenware vessel blocking the opening to the second story, and <b>a woman</b> is standing upstairs <b>kneading</b> dough <b>in a</b> metal <b>bowl,</b> and the <b>woman and the bowl are impure</b> for <b>seven</b> days owing to the impurity of the corpse, <b>while</b> the <b>dough</b> inside the trough <b>is pure?</b> For that would be the result according to Beit Shammai, who distinguishes between food and earthenware vessels on the one hand and metal vessels on the other. Similarly: Is it possible that there is <b>a</b> metal <b>pitcher [<i>login</i>] full of liquid</b> in the second story, and the <b>pitcher</b> should be <b>impure with impurity of seven</b> days, <b>while the liquids</b> remain <b>pure?</b>
After Rabbi Yehoshua posed this question, <b>one student from among the students of Beit Shammai approached him</b> and <b>said to him: I will tell you Beit Shammai’s reasoning. He said to him: Speak. He said to him: Does an impure vessel serve as a barrier</b> to corpse contamination <b>or does it not serve as a barrier?</b> Rabbi Yehoshua <b>said to him: It does not serve as a barrier.</b> The student asked further: And <b>is a vessel of an <i>am ha’aretz</i> pure or impure? He said to him: Impure.</b> The student responded: <b>And if you tell him</b> that his vessel <b>is impure, will he pay attention to you at all?</b> Clearly he will not. <b>What is more, if you say to him</b> that it is <b>impure, he will say to you:</b> On the contrary, <b>my</b> vessel <b>is pure and yours is impure.</b>
<b>And that is Beit Shammai’s reasoning:</b> Food, drink, and earthenware vessels inside a sealed earthenware vessel remain pure, as, since they belong to an <i>am ha’aretz</i>, a <i>ḥaver</i> will not eat the food or borrow the earthenware vessel. Vessels of metal or similar materials may one day be borrowed by a <i>ḥaver</i>, however, and therefore Beit Shammai declared these to be impure.
Once he heard the logic behind Beit Shammai’s opinion, <b>Rabbi Yehoshua immediately went and prostrated himself on the graves of Beit Shammai,</b> i.e., the students and proponents of Shammai, and <b>said: I humble myself before you, bones of Beit Shammai. If such</b> clarity and wisdom is <b>found in your</b> rulings that you stated and left <b>unexplained, all the more so</b> must this be the case <b>in your</b> rulings when they were stated and <b>explained.</b> People <b>said</b> of Rabbi Yehoshua: <b>Throughout his days his teeth darkened because of</b> all <b>his fasts</b> that he undertook to atone for having spoken inappropriately of Beit Shammai.
The Gemara returns to its main point. <b>In any event,</b> this mishna <b>teaches</b> that the status of the vessels of an <i>am ha’aretz</i> is relevant both <b>for you and for him. Apparently,</b> then, <b>we</b> <i>ḥaverim</i> <b>may borrow</b> vessels <b>from</b> <i>amei ha’aretz</i>. The question therefore arises: Why are the Sages not concerned that <i>amei ha’aretz</i> may immerse vessels inside of other vessels in an inappropriate way, so that they will remain unpurified when borrowed by a <i>ḥaver</i>? The Gemara answers: <b>For when we</b> <i>ḥaverim</i> <b>borrow</b> vessels <b>from them we immerse them</b> before using them. It is therefore inconsequential to us if their vessels were not immersed properly beforehand.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so, let Beit Hillel respond to Beit Shammai.</b> They can respond as follows: <b>When we borrow</b> vessels <b>from them we immerse them,</b> and that is why we rule that vessels of metal or similar materials are pure. The Gemara explains: That dispute is referring to the impurity of a corpse. <b>And that which becomes impure by</b> proximity to <b>a corpse requires sprinkling</b> of the red heifer’s ashes on the <b>third and seventh</b> days of its purification, <b>and people do not</b> generally <b>lend vessels for seven days.</b> The solution the <i>ḥaver</i> implements of immersing vessels that he borrows from an <i>am ha’aretz</i> is effective only for other impurities, but not for the impurity imparted by a corpse.
The Gemara poses a question with regard to the <i>halakha</i> that a <i>ḥaver</i> must immerse vessels that he borrows from an <i>am ha’aretz</i>: <b>But</b> is it so that <i>amei ha’aretz</i> <b>are not trusted with regard to immersion? Isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b><i>Amei ha’aretz</i> are trusted with regard to the purification</b> process <b>of immersion of that which has become impure by</b> contact with <b>a corpse?</b> In addition to being sprinkled with purification water on the third and seventh days, a person or article that has been in contact with a corpse must also undergo immersion on the seventh day. An <i>am ha’aretz</i> is believed when he says that he has performed this immersion.
The Gemara presents two answers for this question. <b>Abaye said</b> one answer: This is <b>not difficult. This</b> <i>baraita</i>, which teaches that an <i>am ha’aretz</i> is trusted, is referring <b>to</b> the immersion of <b>his body,</b> whereas <b>this</b> teaching of the Gemara that <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are not trusted concerning immersion deals <b>with his vessels. Rava said</b> a different answer: Both <b>this and that,</b> both the <i>baraita</i> and the Gemara’s teaching, refer <b>to</b> the <b>vessels</b> of an <i>am ha’aretz</i>, <b>and it is</b> nevertheless <b>not difficult. This</b> <i>baraita</i>, which teaches that an <i>am ha’aretz</i> is trusted, is referring to an <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>who said: I never immersed one vessel inside another,</b> which is a statement that we accept. <b>And this</b> teaching of the Gemara that <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are not trusted deals with one <b>who said: I have immersed</b> vessels inside of other vessels, <b>but I did not immerse with a vessel whose mouth does not have</b> the width of <b>the tube of a wineskin.</b> It is with regard to such details that an <i>am ha’aretz</i> cannot be trusted.
<b>And so it was taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> to this effect: <b>An <i>am ha’aretz</i> is trusted to say</b> that <b>produce has not been made susceptible</b> to impurity, i.e., that it has never come into contact with water, <b>but he is not trusted to say</b> that the <b>produce has been made susceptible</b> to impurity <b>but has not</b> actually <b>become impure.</b> This <i>baraita</i> shows that <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are trusted concerning basic facts, but not concerning matters that require detailed knowledge and scrupulous care.
The Gemara poses a question with regard to Abaye’s opinion: <b>And is</b> an <i>am ha’aretz</i> really <b>trusted</b> concerning <b>his body,</b> when he claims to have immersed? <b>Isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: Concerning <b>a <i>ḥaver</i> who comes</b> before those in charge of sprinkling water of purification <b>to be sprinkled</b> with that water, and claims that the requisite three days have passed since his contamination by a corpse, <b>they may sprinkle upon him immediately. But</b> concerning <b>an <i>am ha’aretz</i> who comes</b> before them and claims that three days have passed, <b>they may not sprinkle upon him until he performs</b> and counts <b>in our presence</b> the <b>third</b> day <b>and</b> the <b>seventh</b> day? This shows that an <i>am ha’aretz</i> is not trusted concerning the purity of his own body.
<b>Rather, Abaye said,</b> modifying his previous explanation: <b>Because of the stringency that you applied to</b> the <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>in his beginning,</b> i.e., at the beginning of the purification process, by not allowing him to purify himself without first ensuring that he has not been in contact with a corpse for three days, <b>you</b> may <b>be lenient with him in his end,</b> in that he is trusted regarding having immersed at the end of the seven days, removing the impurity contracted through contact with a corpse.
§ The mishna teaches: The <i>halakhot</i> of <b>the back</b> of a vessel <b>and</b> its <b>inside</b> apply to vessels used for <i>teruma</i>, but not for sacrificial food. The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the meaning of: <b>The back and</b> its <b>inside?</b>
The Gemara explains. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Keilim</i> 25:6): <b>A vessel whose back</b> part, as opposed to its inside, <b>was defiled by</b> contact with impure <b>liquid. Its back is impure,</b> while its other parts, such as <b>its inside, its rim, its ear-shaped handles, and its</b> straight <b>handles are pure.</b> By Torah law, foods and liquids cannot impart ritual impurity to a vessel at all, but by rabbinic law liquids can. In order to clarify that it is only a rabbinic decree, they instituted that the impurity thereby imparted, if the liquid touched the outside of the vessel, should affect only the part touched by the liquid, but not its inside or the other parts of its outside. However, if <b>its inside was defiled,</b> even by impurity only according to rabbinic law, <b>it is all impure.</b> This <i>halakha</i> applies only to <i>teruma</i>, but regarding offerings, the defilement of any part of the vessel renders it all impure.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And its place for gripping.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the meaning of: <b>Place for gripping [<i>beit hatzevita</i>]? Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Shmuel said:</b> It is the <b>place</b> where <b>he</b> grips the vessel in order to <b>pass [<i>tzovet</i>]</b> it, a kind of indentation used for grasping the vessel. <b>And similarly it states: “And he pinched [<i>vayitzbat</i>] some parched corn for her”</b> (Ruth 2:14), which means that he gave her a little of the corn. Therefore, the term is referring to a place on the vessel used to grasp it. <b>Rabbi Asi</b> said that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> It is the <b>place that fastidious people</b> use for <b>dipping.</b> A small receptacle for spices and the like would be attached to the sides of vessels for dipping one’s food.
<b>Rav Beivai taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i> <b>before Rav Naḥman: No vessels have</b> this difference between <b>the back and inside, whether</b> they are vessels used for <b>consecrated</b> foods <b>of the Temple or</b> those used for <b>consecrated</b> foods <b>of outlying areas,</b> i.e., outside the Temple. In these cases, if one of the parts of the vessel was defiled with impure liquids the entire vessel becomes impure. Rav Naḥman <b>said to him: What are these consecrated</b> foods <b>of outlying areas</b> mentioned in the <i>baraita</i>? This term is usually applied to <b><i>teruma</i>,</b> but if so the <i>baraita</i> contradicts the mishna. For <b>didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna: The <i>halakhot</i> of <b>the back</b> of a vessel <b>and</b> its <b>inside and its place for gripping</b> apply <b>to</b> vessels used for <b><i>teruma</i>?</b>
Rav Naḥman continued: <b>Perhaps</b> when you said the consecrated foods of outlying areas you were not referring to <i>teruma</i>, but rather <b>you are speaking of non-sacred</b> food <b>prepared according to the</b> standards of <b>purity of sacrificial</b> food, and you called them consecrated foods of outlying areas because this level of purity can be observed outside the Temple as well.
In the course of this discussion Rav Naḥman said to Rav Beivai: <b>You have</b> now <b>reminded me</b> of something that <b>Rabba bar Avuh said</b> concerning this issue: <b>They taught eleven stringencies</b> of sacrificial food <b>here</b> in the mishna. The <b>first six</b> apply <b>both to sacrificial</b> food <b>and to non-sacred</b> food <b>prepared according to the</b> standards of <b>purity of sacrificial</b> food, whereas <b>the last</b> five <b>apply</b> only <b>to sacrificial</b> food<b>s but not to non-sacred</b> food <b>prepared according to the</b> standards of <b>purity of sacrificial</b> food. Rabba bar Avuh’s statement therefore corroborates the interpretation of the <i>baraita</i> as dealing with non-sacred food prepared according to the standards of purity of sacrificial foods.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>One who carries an object trodden</b> by a <i>zav</i> may <b>carry <i>teruma</i></b> at the same time, if he is careful that neither he nor the impure object come into contact with the <i>teruma</i>, <b>but</b> this may <b>not</b> be done with <b>sacrificial</b> food. The Gemara asks: Concerning <b>sacrificial</b> food, <b>what is the reason</b> that he may <b>not</b> carry it? As long as contact with the food is prevented, why should he not carry sacrificial food as well? The Gemara answers: This enactment was made <b>due to an incident that occurred. As Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Shmuel said:</b> There was once <b>an incident involving someone who was transferring a barrel of sacrificial wine from one place to another,</b>

Daf 23a

<b>and the strap of his sandal,</b> which had been rendered ritually impure by being trodden by a <i>zav</i>, <b>broke off, and he picked up</b> the strap <b>and placed it on</b> top of <b>the mouth of</b> the <b>barrel, and</b> it <b>fell into the airspace of the barrel,</b> thereby <b>rendering</b> the entire barrel <b>impure. At that time</b> the Sages <b>said</b> and issued a permanent decree that <b>one who carries an object that is impure by having been trodden</b> by a <i>zav</i> <b>may carry <i>teruma</i></b> at the same time, <b>but not sacrificial</b> food.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> if they prohibited doing so due to that incident, they should have made the same decree with regard to <b><i>teruma</i> also,</b> because if the same incident would occur with <i>teruma</i> it would defile the <i>teruma</i> as well. The Gemara answers: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> stated? <b>It is</b> that of <b>Rabbi Ḥananya ben Akavya, who said</b> concerning a similar issue: <b>They prohibited</b> it <b>only in the Jordan</b> River, <b>and</b> only <b>in a boat,</b> in a situation <b>similar to the incident that occurred,</b> which triggered the enactment. In his opinion, whenever the Sages enacted a decree prohibiting something due to a particular incident, they did not extend the prohibition to related cases, but only to the same set of circumstances that pertained to that specific incident.
The Gemara presents the background to Rabbi Ḥananya ben Akavya’s statement: <b>What is that</b> enactment that Rabbi Ḥananya ben Akavya was referring to? <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A person may not carry the water of purification and the ashes of purification and transport them across the Jordan</b> River, <b>and</b> this is if he is <b>on a boat. Nor may he stand on one side</b> of the river <b>and throw them to the other side. Nor may he float them</b> across the river. <b>Nor may he</b> ford the river <b>riding on an animal or on his friend</b> and carrying the water or ashes of purification, <b>unless his feet are touching the ground</b> as he fords the river. <b>But he may transfer them</b> across the river <b>over a bridge without concern</b> about transferring them improperly. This decree applies <b>both</b> to <b>the Jordan and</b> to <b>other rivers. Rabbi Ḥananya ben Akavya says:</b> The Sages <b>prohibited</b> these acts <b>only in the Jordan</b> River, <b>and</b> only if he transports them <b>in a boat, and</b> in circumstances exactly <b>like</b> those of <b>the incident that occurred.</b>
The Gemara inquires: <b>What</b> was the <b>incident that occurred? Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> There was once <b>an incident involving a person who was transferring water of purification and ashes of purification in the Jordan, and</b> he was <b>on a boat, and an olive-bulk</b> from a <b>corpse was discovered stuck in the floor of the boat,</b> over which the water of purification had passed, thereby rendering them impure and invalid. <b>At that time</b> the Sages <b>said: A person may not carry water of purification and ashes of purification and transport them across the Jordan,</b> and this is if he is <b>on a boat.</b> Just as in that case, Rabbi Ḥananya ben Akavya stated that the decree was limited to the specific circumstances of the original incident, here too, he would say that since the original incident involved sacrificial food and not <i>teruma</i> the Sages did not apply the decree to <i>teruma</i>.
Several <b>dilemmas were raised before</b> the scholars: It is clear that this <i>halakha</i>, like the incident itself, applies to an object that has actually contracted impurity by being trodden by a <i>zav</i>, such as an <b>impure sandal.</b> But if it is <b>a pure sandal, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is the decree so far-reaching as to include a prohibition against carrying even a pure sandal together with sacrificial food? Similarly, the <i>halakha</i> certainly applies to <b>an open barrel,</b> where there is a danger that the sandal may enter the airspace of the barrel, as in the original incident, but if it was <b>a closed barrel,</b> where there is no such danger, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Does the decree apply in this case as well? Another question: If someone <b>transgressed</b> this decree <b>and carried</b> an impure sandal together with sacrificial food, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Did the Sages decree that the sacrificial food would become defiled because their decree was transgressed or not?
The Gemara presents a conclusion for the last dilemma. <b>Rabbi Ila said: If one transgressed and carried</b> sacrificial food together with an impure sandal, <b>it is</b> indeed rendered <b>impure. Rabbi Zeira said:</b> If <b>he transgressed and carried</b> sacrificial food in this manner, <b>it is</b> still <b>pure.</b>
§ The mishna states: <b>Vessels that were</b> fashioned and <b>completed in purity</b> require immersion for sacrificial food but not for <i>teruma</i>. The Gemara asks: <b>Who completed</b> the work of these vessels? <b>If we say that</b> it was <b>a <i>ḥaver</i>,</b> an individual who is meticulous about the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual purity, who <b>completed them, why</b> should <b>they</b> require <b>immersion,</b> given that he is meticulous about the <i>halakhot</i> of purity?
<b>Rather,</b> it must mean <b>that an <i>am ha’aretz</i> completed them.</b> But can one <b>call these</b> vessels <b>completed in purity?</b> The very touch of an <i>am ha’aretz</i> renders them impure. <b>Rabba bar Sheila said</b> that <b>Rav Mattana said</b> that <b>Shmuel said: Actually,</b> the mishna is dealing with vessels <b>that a <i>ḥaver</i> completed,</b> and the Sages declared them impure for sacrificial food <b>due to</b> a concern that the <b>spittle of an <i>am ha’aretz</i></b> might have fallen on them, which would render them impure if he were a <i>zav</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>When did</b> this hypothetical spittle <b>fall</b> on the vessel? <b>If we say</b> it was <b>before he completed</b> the vessel, in <b>that</b> case <b>it is not</b> yet <b>a vessel,</b> and therefore cannot contract impurity at that stage at all. <b>Rather,</b> it must have sprayed onto the vessel <b>after he completed it.</b> But at that point, since he is a <i>ḥaver</i>, <b>he is careful about it.</b> He would ensure that no spittle would fall on it, so there is no danger that it might have become defiled. The Gemara responds: <b>Actually,</b> we must explain that the spittle fell <b>before he completed it,</b> when the <i>ḥaver</i> was not yet guarding it against impurity, <b>and</b> the Sages were concerned that <b>perhaps at the moment when he completed</b> the vessel the spittle <b>was still moist,</b> and thus still capable of defiling the finished vessel, for the continued presence of the spittle on the vessel might have escaped the attention of the <i>ḥaver</i> craftsman.
The Gemara comments: The mishna states that the vessel requires immersion, implying: <b>Immersion, yes,</b> but <b>the setting of the sun</b> after immersion, <b>not,</b> i.e., it is considered pure immediately after immersion, even though generally the purification engendered by immersion does not take effect until sunset.
The Gemara asks: If so, <b>the mishna is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer. As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Para</i> 5:4): Regarding <b>a tube</b> of reed, <b>which one cut for</b> storing ashes of <b>purification, Rabbi Eliezer says: He should immerse</b> it <b>immediately; Rabbi Yehoshua says: He should</b> first <b>render</b> it <b>impure</b> by touching it to an actual source of impurity <b>and</b> only <b>after that immerse</b> it. <b>And we discussed</b> the mishna’s case, asking: <b>Who cut</b> this tube? <b>If we say a <i>ḥaver</i> cut it, why do I</b> need <b>immersion?</b> The <i>ḥaver</i> prepared it with the strictest adherence to the <i>halakhot</i> of purity. <b>Rather,</b> it must be that an <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>cut it.</b> But <b>in that case, would Rabbi Yehoshua say</b> that <b>he should</b> first <b>render</b> it <b>impure and</b> only then <b>immerse</b> it? <b>Isn’t it already impure,</b> due to the handling of the <i>am ha’aretz</i>?
<b>And</b> in response to this question, <b>Rabba bar Sheila said</b> that <b>Rav Mattana said</b> that <b>Shmuel said: Actually,</b> the case is <b>that a <i>ḥaver</i> cut it, and</b> the reason it requires immersion is <b>because</b> the Sages were concerned that the <b>spittle of an <i>am ha’aretz</i></b> might have touched it. The Gemara asks: <b>When did</b> this hypothetical spittle <b>fall</b> on the tube? <b>If we say</b> it was <b>before he cut</b> the tube, in <b>that</b> case <b>it is not</b> yet <b>a vessel</b> and therefore cannot contract impurity at that stage. <b>Rather,</b> it must have sprayed onto the vessel <b>after he completed it.</b> But at that point, since he is a <i>ḥaver</i>, <b>he is careful about it</b> and would ensure that no spittle would fall on it, so there is no danger that it might have become impure. The Gemara responds: <b>Actually</b> we must explain that the spittle fell <b>before he cut it, and</b> the Sages were concerned that <b>perhaps at the moment when he cut</b> the tube the spittle <b>was still moist,</b> and thus still capable of rendering the finished tube impure.
The Gemara continues: <b>Granted, according to Rabbi Yehoshua it is</b> found <b>that there is</b> an act of <b>recognition</b> performed in opposition <b>to</b> the <b>Sadducees,</b> in order to demonstrate that the Sages do not take their opinions into account.
<b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Para</i> 3:7): <b>They would render impure the priest who burns the</b> red <b>heifer, in order to counter the opinion of</b> the <b>Sadducees, who used to say:</b> The ritual of the red heifer <b>was</b> to be <b>performed by those who had experienced sunset</b> after their immersion. The Sadducee opinion was that all those involved in the preparation of the red heifer must be completely pure, having undergone immersion as well as having waited until after sunset, when the purification takes full effect. The Sages, however, maintain that the red heifer may be prepared by people immediately after immersion, without waiting for sunset. In order to clearly demonstrate their rejection of the Sadducee opinion they would deliberately defile the people involved with preparing the red heifer and then immerse them. It is for this reason as well that Rabbi Yehoshua ruled that the tube must be rendered impure before it is immersed.
<b>But according to Rabbi Eliezer, granted, if you say</b> that <b>generally, the setting of the sun is required</b> after a vessel is completed, <b>it is</b> found <b>that there is</b> some act of <b>recognition</b> in opposition <b>to</b> the <b>Sadducees,</b> as according to him, the tube used for the red heifer ashes, which should normally have required the setting of the sun, is used without waiting for sunset. <b>But if you say</b> that <b>generally the setting of the sun is not required</b> to remove the impurity caused by the touch of an <i>am ha’aretz</i>, and immersion alone is sufficient, <b>what</b> act of <b>recognition</b> in opposition <b>to</b> the <b>Sadducees is there</b> here? It must be, therefore, that Rabbi Eliezer requires the setting of the sun for purification from the impurity imparted by an <i>am ha’aretz</i>. Therefore, the mishna, which implies that immersion alone is sufficient, does not accord with Rabbi Eliezer’s opinion.
<b>Rav said:</b> Indeed, Rabbi Eliezer does not require the setting of the sun for vessels that have been completed in purity, but there is nevertheless a demonstrative sign that the opinion of the Sadducees is rejected,

Daf 23b

as <b>they treated</b> the tube with severity <b>as</b> though it had been <b>rendered impure</b> by contact with a dead <b>creeping animal.</b> Therefore, in not requiring the setting of the sun they made it noticeable that they were opposed to the Sadducees.
The Gemara raises a difficulty. <b>However, if</b> that is <b>so,</b> that the tube is treated as if defiled by a creeping animal, <b>it should not render a person impure,</b> as something defiled by a creeping animal is impure to the first degree of ritual impurity, which cannot impart ritual impurity to people. <b>Why,</b> then, <b>is it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The one who <b>cuts and immerses</b> the tube for the red heifer ashes <b>requires immersion</b> himself, which shows that the tube does defile people? <b>But rather,</b> say that <b>they treated it as</b> something <b>made impure by</b> contact with <b>a corpse,</b> for such objects do defile people as well. The Gemara asks: <b>If that is so,</b> the tube <b>should</b> also <b>require the sprinkling</b> of the water of purification on the <b>third and seventh</b> days of its purification, like all things defiled by a corpse.
<b>Why,</b> then, <b>is it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The one who <b>cuts and immerses it requires immersion,</b> which indicates that <b>immersion</b> for the tube, <b>yes,</b> this is required, but <b>sprinkling of the third and the seventh</b> day, <b>no. Rather,</b> you must say that <b>they treated it as</b> something <b>made impure by</b> contact with <b>a corpse</b> that is already <b>in its seventh</b> day, after its sprinklings, when it is still impure and imparts impurity to those who touch it, but requires only immersion and no further sprinkling.
The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The Sages <b>never made any innovations</b> in the <i>halakhot</i> of impurity <b>with regard to the</b> red <b>heifer</b> procedure. In other words, although the Sages added stringencies to the red heifer procedure, they never created new <i>halakhot</i> for it that do not exist elsewhere in other areas of <i>halakha</i>. We have said that the tube is treated as if it had had contact with a corpse even though it did not; this is an innovation that is not found anywhere else.
