The Gemara answers: <b>Abaye said:</b> When they said that the Sages did not make innovations, they were not referring to something of this nature, but meant that <b>they did not say</b> that <b>a spade</b> upon which a <i>zav</i> sits <b>can become impure as a seat.</b> They thereby preserved the basic <i>halakhot</i> of impurity, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: It states with regard to a <i>zav</i>: <b>“And he who sits on any object</b> whereon the <i>zav</i> sits shall wash his clothes and bathe himself in water, and be unclean until the evening” (Leviticus 15:6). One <b>might</b> have thought that if a <i>zav</i> <b>turned over</b> a vessel used to measure <b>a <i>se’a</i> and sat on it,</b> or if he turned over a vessel used to measure <b>a <i>tarkav</i>,</b> i.e., a half-<i>se’a</i>, <b>and sat on it,</b> that the vessel <b>should be</b> rendered <b>impure</b> as a seat upon which a <i>zav</i> sat.
The <i>baraita</i> concludes: Therefore, <b>the verse states: “And he who sits on any object whereon</b> the <i>zav</i> <b>sits…and be unclean</b> until the evening” (Leviticus 15:6). The wording of the verse indicates that it is speaking of an object <b>that is designated for sitting,</b> i.e., upon which people generally sit, <b>excluding such</b> a vessel for <b>which we would say to</b> someone sitting on it: <b>Stand up, so we can do our work.</b> A spade and a measuring bowl, then, are not subject to the impurity of the seat of a <i>zav</i>, even if a <i>zav</i> sat on them. Rather, they are considered to be on the lower level of impurity transmitted through simple contact with a <i>zav</i>. The same <i>halakha</i> was applied to the vessels used in preparation of the red heifer; the Sages did not add stringency and decree that a vessel not generally used for sitting should be considered as the seat of a <i>zav</i>.
§ The mishna states: <b>A vessel combines</b> all the food that is in it with regard <b>to sacrificial</b> food <b>but not</b> with regard <b>to <i>teruma</i>.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? What is the source for this stringency? <b>Rabbi Ḥanin said: The verse states</b> with regard to the sacrificial donations of the tribal princes: <b>“One golden pan of ten shekels, full of incense”</b> (Numbers 7:14), which teaches us that <b>the verse treats everything inside the pan</b> as <b>one</b> unit, even if the items are not attached to each other.
<b>Rav Kahana raised an objection</b> against this based on the mishna that teaches (<i>Eduyyot</i> 8:1): <b>Rabbi Akiva added</b> to the list of items that are considered combined when in the same vessel <b>fine flour, incense, frankincense, and coals,</b> saying <b>that if one who immersed himself that day</b> but has not waited until sunset <b>touched</b> a <b>part</b> of the contents of a vessel containing these substances, <b>all of</b> the vessel’s contents <b>are disqualified,</b> as the vessel combines them.
<b>Isn’t this</b> <i>halakha</i> that these substances are considered combined <b>by rabbinic law? From where</b> do we know that this is so? <b>From</b> the fact <b>that it teaches</b> in <b>the first clause</b> of that mishna: <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Beteira testified with regard to ashes of purification</b> in a vessel <b>that</b> if <b>an impure</b> person or object <b>touched part of it, it renders all of it impure.</b> The purification ashes of the red heifer are neither food nor a sacrificial item, so Rabbi Ḥanin’s verse does not apply to the ashes, and the vessel certainly does not combine the ashes together by Torah law, but by rabbinic law. <b>And it is taught</b> immediately following this: <b>Rabbi Akiva added,</b> which shows that Rabbi Akiva’s <i>halakha</i>, like the previous <i>halakha</i>, deals with an additional level of impurity instituted by the Sages rather than a Torah law.
<b>Reish Lakish said in the name of Bar Kappara:</b>

Daf 24a

Rabbi Akiva’s testimony is not needed to teach the basic <i>halakha</i> that a vessel combines its ingredients, which is Torah law; <b>it is necessary only for the remainders of the meal-offering,</b> the part of a meal-offering left over after a fistful of it and its frankincense have been sacrificed on the altar, which is eaten by a priest. In such a case the <i>halakha</i> of combining applies only by rabbinic law, for <b>by Torah law</b> only when an item <b>requires a vessel</b> in order for it to be sanctified does <b>the vessel combine it</b> with regard to impurity, even if its parts are not touching each other. But in the case of something <b>that does not require a vessel,</b> the <b>vessel does not combine it.</b> The remainder of a meal-offering no longer requires a vessel, since it is given to a priest after the fistful is sacrificed, so the flour in a vessel would not be considered combined according to Torah law.
<b>And the Sages came and decreed that even if</b> something <b>does not require a vessel,</b> such as the leftover flour of the meal-offering, the <b>vessel</b> nevertheless <b>combines it.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: It <b>works out well</b> in the case of <b>flour,</b> which can be said to refer to the flour left over from meal-offerings, but with regard to <b>incense and frankincense, what is there to say?</b> In these cases a vessel is certainly required, but if the <i>halakha</i> of combining applies to them from the Torah, why did Rabbi Akiva include them in his list? <b>Rav Naḥman said</b> that <b>Rabba bar Avuh said: For example, if he piled them up on a leather board [<i>kartavla</i>],</b> rather than in a containing vessel. <b>By Torah law</b> a vessel that <b>has an inside combines</b> its ingredients, but <b>one that</b> is flat and <b>does not have an inside does not combine. And the Sages came and decreed that even if it does not have an inside it</b> nevertheless <b>combines</b> what is placed on it.
The Gemara comments: <b>And</b> this opinion <b>of Rabbi Ḥanin’s,</b> that impurity by combining is derived from the Torah, <b>disagrees with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba. For Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said</b> in reference to our mishna: <b>This mishna was taught based on Rabbi Akiva’s testimony.</b> In other words, the mishna’s teaching that a vessel combines its contents follows the statement of Rabbi Akiva, indicating that it is by rabbinic law, unlike Rabbi Ḥanin, who said that it is based on a source from the Torah.
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>The fourth degree</b> of impurity, <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> food, is <b>disqualified. It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yosei said: From where</b> is it derived with regard <b>to the fourth degree</b> of ritual impurity, that <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> food it <b>is disqualified? It is a logical derivation,</b> by <i>a fortiori</i>: <b>If one who is lacking atonement,</b> an impure person who is obligated to bring an offering to complete his purification process, <b>who is permitted to</b> eat <b><i>teruma</i>,</b> is nevertheless <b>disqualified with regard to</b> the consumption of <b>sacrificial</b> food, as specified in the Torah, then concerning something that is impure to the <b>third</b> degree of ritual impurity, <b>which is disqualified</b> if it is <b><i>teruma</i>, is it not right that it should engender a fourth</b> degree of ritual impurity when it touches <b>sacrificial</b> food? Therefore, <b>we have learned</b> that there is a <b>third</b> degree of impurity with regard <b>to sacrificial food from the Torah, and</b> that there is a <b>fourth</b> degree of impurity <b>from <i>a fortiori</i></b> reasoning.
The above <i>baraita</i> taught that there is a <b>third</b> degree of impurity <b>for sacrificial</b> food <b>from the Torah.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> is this derived? <b>As it is written: “And the meat that touches any impure thing shall not be eaten”</b> (Leviticus 7:19). <b>Is it not</b> so that <b>we are not dealing</b> in that verse with meat that touches any “impure thing” at all, even <b>if it touched</b> something that is of the <b>second</b> degree of ritual impurity, which is also called an “impure thing”? <b>And</b> yet <b>the Merciful One states</b> with regard to that meat, which having touched a second-degree impurity is now impure to the third degree: <b>“It shall not be eaten,”</b> meaning that it has been rendered unfit due to impurity. And as for the <i>baraita’s</i> statement that the <b>fourth</b> level of impurity is derived <b>by an <i>a fortiori</i> inference –</b> it is <b>as we said</b> just above, the <i>a fortiori</i> inference put forth by Rabbi Yosei.
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>And with regard to <i>teruma</i>, if</b> one of one’s hands <b>became impure</b> with impurity by rabbinic law that renders only the hands impure, its counterpart, the other hand, remains pure. But with regard to sacrificial food, if one hand becomes impure he must immerse them both. <b>Rav Sheizevi said:</b> When they said that with regard to sacrificial food a hand that is rendered impure renders the other hand impure as well, <b>they taught</b> this only for a situation <b>when</b> the pure hand is <b>in contact</b> with the sacrificial food when the impure hand touches it. <b>But</b> if the pure hand is <b>not in contact</b> with the sacrificial food, <b>no,</b> the pure hand is not rendered impure by touching the impure hand. According to Rav Sheizevi, the Sages enacted the decree that one hand renders the other impure because they were concerned that the impure hand may have touched the sacrificial food directly without being noticed. Therefore, the decree applies only when the pure hand is touching the sacrificial food.
<b>Abaye raised an objection to</b> Rav Sheizevi from the following teaching: Even <b>a dry hand</b> that is impure <b>renders its counterpart,</b> i.e., the other hand, <b>impure, to</b> the extent that the second hand will now <b>render impure</b> any food that it touches. This is true with regard <b>to sacrificial</b> food <b>but not</b> with regard <b>to <i>teruma</i>.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says:</b> The second hand is not rendered impure to such a severe extent. It can merely <b>disqualify</b> sacrificial food that it touches, by rendering it impure to the fourth degree, <b>but not render</b> it <b>impure</b> with third-degree impurity.
<b>Granted, if you say</b> that the second hand becomes impure even <b>when it is not in contact</b> with the sacrificial food, <b>this</b> would explain <b>the noteworthiness of a dry</b> hand rendering its counterpart impure. It teaches that even though normally a dry hand would not render another hand impure, the Sages nevertheless declared it impure with regard to sacrificial food. <b>But if you say</b> that when the second hand is <b>in contact</b> with sacrificial food, <b>yes,</b> the decree that the second hand becomes impure applies, lest the impure hand touch the sacrificial food directly, but <b>when it is not in contact, no,</b> the decree does not apply, then <b>what is the noteworthiness</b> of stating that it applies in the case of a <b>dry</b> hand?
<b>It was also stated</b> that <i>amora’im</i> disputed a similar issue: <b>Reish Lakish said: They taught</b> that one hand renders the other impure <b>only</b> if the second hand is <b>his</b> own <b>hand,</b>

Daf 24b

<b>but</b> an impure hand does <b>not</b> render impure <b>the hand of another. And Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> An impure hand renders another hand impure <b>whether it is his</b> own <b>hand or the hand of another,</b> provided the second hand is touched <b>by the same hand</b> that came into contact with the impurity. Moreover, the impure hand affects the food it touches only <b>to disqualify</b> it, <b>but not to render</b> it <b>impure.</b>
The Gemara elaborates on Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion. <b>From where</b> did he learn this? <b>From</b> the fact <b>that it teaches</b> in <b>a latter clause</b> in the mishna: <b>For one renders its counterpart,</b> i.e., the other hand, <b>impure</b> with regard <b>to sacrificial</b> food <b>but not</b> with regard <b>to <i>teruma</i>. Why do I need this</b> phrase <b>as well? It was</b> already <b>taught in the first clause</b> that one hand renders the other hand impure with regard to sacrificial food. <b>Rather,</b> must one <b>not conclude from</b> this added phrase that it comes <b>to include</b> the rendering impure of <b>the hand of another</b> as well as his own other hand?
The Gemara observes: <b>And Reish Lakish, too, retracted</b> his own opinion in favor of Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion. For <b>Rabbi Yona said</b> that <b>Rabbi Ami said</b> that <b>Reish Lakish said:</b> The decree that one hand renders another impure applies <b>whether it is his</b> own <b>hand or the hand of another,</b> provided the second hand is touched <b>by the same hand</b> that came into contact with the impurity. Moreover, the impure hand affects the food it touches only <b>to disqualify</b> it, <b>but not to render</b> it <b>impure.</b>
The Gemara comments: <b>And</b> this opinion, that an impure hand affects the sacrificial food only <b>to disqualify</b> it <b>but not to render</b> it <b>impure, is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>. As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Yadayim</i> 3:2): <b>Anything that disqualifies <i>teruma</i></b> by contact with it, i.e., anything that is impure at least to the second degree, <b>renders the hands impure</b> to the <b>second</b> degree. <b>And</b> furthermore, <b>a hand</b> that is impure to the second degree <b>renders its counterpart,</b> the other hand, <b>impure;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehoshua. But the Rabbis say: Hands</b> themselves <b>are</b> impure to the <b>second</b> degree, <b>and</b> that which is impure to the <b>second</b> degree <b>cannot impart</b> second-degree impurity to something else <b>with regard to non-sacred</b> food.
From the Rabbis’ response to Rabbi Yehoshua it is clear that the latter’s opinion is that the second hand is indeed rendered impure to the second degree, imparting third-degree impurity to sacrificial food that it touches. Moreover: <b>What, is it not</b> correct to infer from the Rabbis’ words that <b>it is</b> impurity to the <b>second</b> degree <b>that</b> the first hand <b>does not impart</b> to the second hand by touching it, <b>but</b> impurity to the <b>third</b> degree it <b>does impart</b> to the second hand? The second hand would thus only disqualify the sacrificial food that it touches by imparting to it a fourth-degree impurity. Therefore, the Rabbis and Rabbi Yehoshua are arguing about this very point: Does the second hand only disqualify sacrificial food by imparting to it fourth-degree impurity, or does it render the food impure with third-degree impurity?
The Gemara rejects this proof: <b>Perhaps</b> the Rabbis meant that the first hand does <b>not impart</b> impurity to the second hand to <b>either the second or the third</b> degree, for in their opinion one impure hand does not defile the other hand at all, in contradiction to the mishna, whereas the opinion expressed in the mishna would be following Rabbi Yehoshua.
<b>Rather,</b> the issue of which degree of impurity is imparted to the second hand is <b>like</b> the following dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i> cited earlier: Even <b>a dry hand</b> that is impure <b>renders its counterpart,</b> i.e., the other hand, <b>impure to</b> the extent that the second hand will now <b>render impure</b> food that it touches. This is true with regard <b>to sacrificial</b> food <b>but not</b> with regard <b>to <i>teruma</i>.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says: The</b> second <b>hand</b> is not rendered impure to such a severe extent. It can merely <b>disqualify</b> sacrificial food that it touches, by making it impure to the fourth degree, <b>but not render</b> it <b>impure</b> to the third degree.
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>One may eat dry foods,</b> i.e., foods that have never been wetted and are thus not susceptible to impurity, <b>with impure hands</b> in the case of <i>teruma</i>, but not in the case of sacrificial food. <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that there is a difficulty with this statement of the mishna: <b>Rabbi Ḥanina ben Antigonus said: Now, is there</b> such a possibility as <b>dry</b> food, i.e., food that is not susceptible to impurity, with regard <b>to sacrificial</b> food? <b>Is it not so that the reverence</b> accorded <b>to sacrificial</b> food itself <b>renders it fit</b> to contract impurity even if it has never been in contact with liquid at all? All sacrificial food is thus automatically susceptible to impurity, and whether it is dry, i.e., it has never been wetted, or not is irrelevant.
The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> it is <b>necessary</b> for the mishna to state this. And the case is not one of dry sacrificial food, as was first assumed. Rather, the mishna is dealing with a case in <b>which, for example, his friend inserted</b> sacrificial food or <i>teruma</i> <b>into his mouth, or,</b> alternatively, a case in <b>which he inserted</b> it <b>into his own</b> mouth <b>by</b> means of <b>a spindle or whorl,</b> which are wooden vessels without receptacles that cannot contract impurity. In either event the food arrives in his mouth without being rendered impure by his impure hands. <b>And</b> then, while the sacrificial food or <i>teruma</i> is still in his mouth, he <b>wishes to</b> put into his mouth and <b>eat a non-sacred radish or onion along with them.</b>
Non-sacred food is not rendered impure by being touched with impure hands, so it should not be problematic to put a radish or onion into one’s mouth with his hands. However, <b>regarding sacrificial food the Sages enacted a decree against</b> doing so, lest the person inadvertently touch the sacrificial food in his mouth with his impure hand. This decree, the mishna teaches, applies only to sacrificial food, <b>but in</b> a case in which he had <b><i>teruma</i></b> in his mouth <b>the Sages did not enact a decree against it.</b>
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>An acute mourner and one who is lacking atonement</b> require immersion in order to eat sacrificial food, but this is not necessary for <i>teruma</i>. The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> for this decree? <b>Since until now it was prohibited</b> for <b>them</b> to partake of sacrificial food and they therefore might not have guarded themselves properly from impurity, <b>the Sages required them</b> to undergo <b>immersion</b> before eating sacrificial food.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The previous mishna listed stringencies that apply to sacrificial food but not to <i>teruma</i>. However, there are also <b>stringencies</b> that apply <b>to <i>teruma</i></b> over sacrificial food: <b>In Judea</b> all people, even people who are not generally meticulous in their observance of the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual purity [<i>amei ha’aretz</i>], <b>are trusted with regard to the purity of</b> consecrated <b>wine and oil</b> throughout <b>all the days of the year. And during the period of the winepress and olive press,</b> when grapes and olives are pressed and made into wine and oil, respectively, they are trusted <b>even with regard to</b> the purity of <b><i>teruma</i>,</b> as all people, including <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, purify their vessels for this season.
But once <b>the</b> periods of the <b>winepress and olive press have passed,</b> if <i>amei ha’aretz</i> <b>brought to him,</b> i.e., to a priest who is meticulous concerning the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual purity [<i>ḥaver</i>], a <b>barrel of <i>teruma</i> wine, he may not accept it from them,</b> as <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are not trusted with regard to matters of ritual purity during the rest of the year. <b>But</b> the giver <b>may leave it</b> over <b>for the following winepress</b> season, in the following year, at which point the <i>ḥaver</i> priest may accept it from him, although it was prohibited for him to accept the same barrel beforehand. <b>And if</b> the giver <b>said to</b> the priest: <b>I separated</b> and placed <b>into</b> this barrel of <i>teruma</i> <b>a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>of sacrificial</b> wine or oil, <b>he is trusted</b> with regard to the entire contents of the barrel. Since an <i>am ha’aretz</i> is trusted with regard to the purity of sacrificial food, he is also believed with regard to <i>teruma</i> that is mingled with the sacrificial food.
With regard to <b>jugs of wine and jugs of oil</b>

Daf 25a

<b>that are mingled,</b> <i>amei ha’aretz</i> <b>are trusted with regard to them during the period of the winepress and the olive press, and</b> also up to <b>seventy days before the winepress,</b> for that is when people begin to purify their vessels in preparation for the wine-pressing season.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are trusted with regard to the purity of sacrificial wine and oil in Judea. The Gemara infers: <b>In Judea, yes, but in the Galilee, no. What is the reason</b> for this distinction between the two places?
<b>Reish Lakish said:</b> It is <b>because a strip</b> of land inhabited <b>by Samaritans [<i>Kutim</i>] separates between</b> Judea and the Galilee, and it is impossible to travel from one land to the other without traversing this strip. The Sages decreed that lands inhabited by non-Jewish nations are considered ritually impure, so that it would be impossible to transport food from the Galilee to Judea, where the Temple is located, without the food becoming impure. Therefore, even oil and wine prepared by <i>ḥaverim</i> who lived in the Galilee were not accepted for sacrificial use.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>And let</b> the residents of the Galilee <b>place</b> the wine and oil and transport it to Judea <b>in a</b> closed <b>box, a chest, or a closet,</b> whose contents cannot contract impurity, as they have the status of separate tents. The Gemara answers: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> mishna? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>who said: A thrown tent,</b> i.e., a moving tent, <b>is not called a</b> proper <b>tent,</b> and therefore its contents are subject to impurity. In our case, then, the contents would contract the impurity decreed upon the lands of non-Jewish nations. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: Concerning <b>one who enters a land of non-Jewish nations</b> sitting <b>in a box, a chest, or a closet, Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>declares</b> him to be <b>impure, and Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, declares</b> him to be <b>pure.</b>
The Gemara raises a further difficulty: <b>And let them bring</b> oil and wine to the Temple <b>in an earthenware vessel sealed</b> with <b>a tightly bound cover,</b> which cannot contract impurity even if it is in the same tent as a corpse, as it states: “And every open vessel, which has no covering tightly bound upon it, is unclean” (Numbers 19:15). <b>Rabbi Eliezer said:</b> The Sages <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Sacrificial</b> food, unlike other items, is <b>not spared</b> from impurity <b>by</b> being in a container with <b>a tightly bound cover.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: Water of <b>purification</b> containing ashes from the red heifer <b>is not spared</b> from impurity <b>by</b> being in a vessel with <b>a tightly bound cover? What, is it not</b> implied in the <i>baraita</i> <b>this</b> inference: That <b>sacrificial</b> food <b>is spared</b> from impurity in such a situation? The <i>baraita</i> seems to imply that this is a special stringency for water of purification, which does not apply to anything else, including sacrificial food. The Gemara rejects this: <b>No,</b> the <i>baraita’s</i> inference should be understood differently, as <b>this: Water that has not</b> yet <b>been consecrated</b> by being mixed with ashes of the red heifer <b>is spared</b> from impurity <b>by</b> being in a vessel with <b>a tightly bound cover,</b> even if they are designated for such a use at a later stage.
The Gemara raises another difficulty: <b>But didn’t Ulla say: <i>Ḥaverim</i> purify</b> their wine and oil, i.e., they produce their wine and oil by the standards of purity used for sacrificial food <b>in the Galilee,</b> to be used for sacrificial purposes? This indicates that there must have been some way of transporting them from the Galilee to the Temple, for otherwise why would they have prepared such items? The Gemara answers: Indeed, they could not transfer these items to the Temple. Rather, they would <b>leave</b> them in their place, <b>and</b> their thought was that <b>when Elijah comes</b> in messianic times and <b>purifies</b> the road from Galilee to Judea, these items will become eligible for use.
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>And during the period of the winepress</b> and olive press, <i>amei ha’aretz</i> <b>are trusted even with regard to</b> the purity of <b><i>teruma</i>. And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from the following teaching: An <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>who finishes</b> pressing <b>his olives should leave over one sack</b> of unpressed olives, <b>and give it to a poor priest</b> as <i>teruma</i>, so that the priest himself can make ritually pure oil from it. This shows that even during the period of the olive press the <i>am ha’aretz</i> is not trusted to make pure olive oil himself.
<b>Rav Naḥman said:</b> This is <b>not difficult. This</b> case of the mishna, where <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are trusted to produce pure olive oil themselves, is referring <b>to</b> people who press their olives <b>early,</b> during the regular season of the olive press, while <b>that</b> case is referring <b>to</b> those who press their olives <b>later,</b> after the period when most people press their olives has passed. <b>Rav Adda bar Ahava said to him:</b> Such as <b>what</b> case, <b>for example?</b> Such <b>as those</b> olives <b>of your father’s house.</b> Rav Naḥman’s father had many olives, and he often pressed them after the regular pressing season.
<b>Rav Yosef said</b> a different resolution of the above contradiction. The source that states that <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are not trusted <b>was taught</b> with <b>regard to the Galilee,</b> and as the mishna taught earlier concerning sacrificial wine and oil, <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are trusted only in Judea and not in the Galilee. <b>Abaye raised an objection to him</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Transjordan and the Galilee are like Judea,</b> in that <b>they are trusted with regard to wine</b> of <i>teruma</i> <b>during the period of wine</b> production, <b>and with regard to oil</b> of <i>teruma</i> <b>during the period of oil</b> production. <b>However,</b> they are <b>not</b> trusted <b>with regard to wine during the period of oil</b> production, <b>nor</b> are they trusted <b>with regard to oil during the period of wine</b> production. This <i>baraita</i> shows that with regard to <i>teruma</i> there is no difference between the trustworthiness of <i>amei ha’aretz</i> who live in the Galilee and that of those who live in Judea.
<b>Rather,</b> Rav Yosef’s answer must be rejected, and <b>it is clear</b> that the correct answer is <b>as we answered initially,</b> that it is speaking of the period following the conclusion of the winepress.
§ It was taught in the mishna: Once <b>the</b> periods of the <b>winepress and olive press have passed,</b> if <i>amei ha’aretz</i> <b>brought to</b> a <i>ḥaver</i> priest a <b>barrel of <i>teruma</i> wine, he may not accept it from them. But</b> the giver <b>may leave it</b> over <b>for the following winepress</b> season, in the following year. <b>They raised a dilemma before Rav Sheshet:</b> If the priest <b>violated</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>and did accept</b> the wine from an <i>am ha’aretz</i>, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is it permissible <b>that he should leave it</b> over for himself <b>for the following winepress</b> season? Since it is permissible to accept the wine and oil of an <i>am ha’aretz</i> intentionally left until that time, perhaps it is also permissible if the priest himself intentionally leaves it over until that time. <b>He said to him: You learned it</b> in a mishna (<i>Demai</i> 6:9):

Daf 25b

In the case of <b>a <i>ḥaver</i> and an <i>am ha’aretz</i>, who</b> are brothers and <b>inherited</b> property from <b>their father,</b> who was also <b>an <i>am ha’aretz</i>,</b> the <i>ḥaver</i> <b>can say to</b> his <i>am ha’aretz</i> brother: <b>You take</b> the <b>wheat that is in such and such a place and I</b> will take the <b>wheat that is in such and such a place.</b> The <i>ḥaver</i> knows that the former batch of wheat had been made susceptible to impurity, and he would therefore have no use for it, while the latter batch had not been made susceptible to impurity.
The mishna continues: Similarly, the <i>ḥaver</i> brother may say to the <i>am ha’aretz</i> brother: <b>You take</b> the <b>wine that is in such and such a place and I</b> will take the <b>wine that is in such and such a place.</b> The <i>ḥaver</i> knows that the latter batch of wine has not been rendered impure, and he wants to take that batch as his share. When brothers inherit a quantity of a certain item they will each end up receiving an equal share of that item. Therefore, the principle of retroactive designation applies, meaning that it is considered that whatever portion any particular brother receives in the end is the one that had been designated for him as his inheritance from the beginning. It is not considered to be a trade or a business transaction with the other brothers in exchange for their portions.
The mishna continues: <b>But</b> the <i>ḥaver</i> brother <b>may not say to</b> the <i>am ha’aretz</i> brother: <b>You take</b> the <b>wet</b> produce <b>and I</b> will take the <b>dry</b> produce. Nor may he say: <b>You take</b> the <b>wheat, and I</b> will take the <b>barley.</b> The principle of retroactive designation does not apply to objects of different types. If one brother would take wheat and the other barley it would be considered a trade. And it is prohibited for a <i>ḥaver</i> to sell or transfer impure produce or produce that is susceptible to impurity to an <i>am ha’aretz</i>, who does not strictly follow the principles of purity, as this would involve the prohibition of “You shall not place a stumbling-block before the blind” (Leviticus 19:14).
<b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>with regard to</b> this mishna: If the <i>ḥaver</i> receives a share that consists of some items that are wet and some that are dry, <b>that <i>ḥaver</i> must burn the wet</b> produce if it was <i>teruma</i>, as it has certainly been defiled and impure <i>teruma</i> is burned, <b>but he may leave the dry</b> produce and use it, as it can be assumed that it has not been defiled.
The Gemara draws a conclusion based on this <i>baraita</i>: <b>Why</b> must he burn the wet <i>teruma</i>? <b>Let him leave it over until the following winepress</b> season, during which time <i>teruma</i> from an <i>am ha’aretz</i> is considered pure. The fact that this option is not taken into account indicates that one may not intentionally leave over <i>teruma</i> received from an <i>am ha’aretz</i> until the next winepress season, which would resolve the dilemma presented to Rav Sheshet. The Gemara rejects this proof: Here it is referring <b>to something that does not have a winepress,</b> i.e., to liquids that are never used in the Temple service, as <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are careful only with regard to liquids that may be used in the Temple service. The Gemara asks: Even so, there is another option: <b>And let him leave it for the</b> next <b>pilgrimage Festival,</b> for the mishna later teaches (26a) that <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are assumed to observe all laws of ritual purity on Festivals. The Gemara answers: It is referring <b>to something that would not last until the</b> next <b>pilgrimage Festival,</b> but which would spoil beforehand. The dilemma presented to Rav Sheshet therefore remains unresolved.
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>And if</b> the giver <b>says</b> to the priest: <b>I separated</b> and placed <b>into</b> this barrel of <i>teruma</i> <b>a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> <b>of sacrificial</b> wine or oil, <b>he is deemed credible.</b> The Gemara proceeds to cite a mishna in tractate <i>Oholot</i>, the discussion of which ultimately relates to this mishna here: <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>elsewhere</b> (<i>Oholot</i> 18:4): <b>Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel agree that one may examine</b> the ground <b>for those performing the paschal</b> offering, <b>but one may not examine</b> the ground <b>for those who eat <i>teruma</i>.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the meaning of: <b>One may examine? Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Shmuel said: A person may blow</b> at the ground of <b>a <i>beit haperas</i> and walk</b> through it as he does so. A <i>beit haperas</i> is a patch of ground with a grave in it that was subsequently plowed over. The Sages were concerned that there might be small pieces of human bone scattered in the field, which would impart impurity to anyone moving them with his foot. Therefore, they decreed that whoever traverses such a field becomes impure. However, the Sages allowed one to pass through the field while maintaining his purity if he blows on the ground as he goes, the assumption being that any small pieces of bone would thereby be blown out of his path. This is the examination to which this mishna refers. The mishna teaches that this examination is sufficient to allow one to retain his purity as he goes to perform the paschal offering, but not to allow one to retain his purity with regard to the eating of <i>teruma</i>.
<b>And Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said in the name of Ulla: A <i>beit haperas</i> that has been trodden</b> by passersby who have beaten a path through it <b>is</b> considered <b>pure for those who</b> are on their way to <b>perform the paschal</b> offering, as the assumption is that no more bone fragments remain on the surface of the ground. The examination referred to in the mishna, then, is referring to ascertaining whether a particular <i>beit haperas</i> has been trodden or not. The reason for this leniency is that the impure status of a <i>beit haperas</i> is only a rabbinical decree, and the Sages <b>did not uphold their words</b> decreeing the field to be impure <b>in a place</b> where this affects one’s ability to perform a mitzva involving <b><i>karet</i>;</b> and failure to bring a paschal offering is punishable by <i>karet</i>.
However, with regard <b>to those who</b> wish to <b>eat <i>teruma</i></b> after traversing a <i>beit haperas</i>, the Sages <b>did uphold their words</b> decreeing the field to be impure <b>in a place</b> of a sin involving the punishment of <b>death</b> by God’s hand. The sin of eating <i>teruma</i> in a state of impurity is punishable by death by God’s hand, and the Sages were therefore strict in insisting that one not eat it after traversing a <i>beit haperas</i>.
<b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the scholars: If <b>one examined a</b> <i>beit haperas</i> <b>for</b> the purpose of bringing <b>his paschal</b> offering, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <i>teruma</i> after he has traversed the field? Is it permissible <b>that he</b> can rely on this examination to <b>eat his <i>teruma</i></b> as well, since his passage through the field has been established as not having defiled him? <b>Ulla said:</b> If he <b>examined</b> the ground <b>for</b> purposes of bringing <b>his paschal</b> offering, <b>he is permitted</b> afterward <b>to eat his <i>teruma</i>,</b> for once he is declared pure with regard to the offerings it would be inconsistent to declare him at the same time impure with regard to <i>teruma</i>. But <b>Rabba bar Ulla said:</b> If one <b>examined</b> the ground <b>for</b> purposes of bringing <b>his paschal</b> offering, it <b>is prohibited</b> for him <b>to eat his <i>teruma</i>.</b>
<b>A certain older man said to</b> Rabba bar Ulla: <b>Do not argue with Ulla, as we learned</b> in the mishna <b>in accordance with his</b> opinion, for the mishna states: <b>And if</b> the <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>says</b> to the priest: <b>I separated</b> and placed <b>into</b> this barrel of <i>teruma</i> <b>a quarter-</b><i>log</i> of <b>sacrificial</b> wine or oil, <b>he is deemed credible. Apparently, then, once</b> the <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>is deemed credible</b> with regard to the <b>sacrificial</b> items in the barrel <b>he is also deemed credible</b> with regard to the <b><i>teruma</i></b> in it. <b>Here too,</b> the same principle should be applied, so <b>once he is deemed credible</b> and is considered pure with regard to the <b>paschal</b> offering, <b>he is also deemed credible with regard to <i>teruma</i>.</b>
§ It was taught in the mishna: Concerning <b>jugs of wine and jugs of oil</b> that are mingled, <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are deemed credible during the period of the winepress and the olive press. A Sage <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <i>Amei ha’aretz</i> <b>are not deemed credible, neither with regard to flasks nor with regard to <i>teruma</i>.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Flasks of what? If</b> it is referring to <b>flasks of sacrificial</b> food, <b>since</b> an <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>is deemed credible</b> concerning the <b>sacrificial</b> items it contains, he must necessarily be <b>deemed credible</b> concerning the <b>flasks as well. Rather,</b> it is referring to <b>flasks of <i>teruma</i>.</b> But this <b>is obvious: Now,</b> if he <b>is not deemed credible</b> concerning the <b><i>teruma</i></b> itself, is it possible that he would be <b>deemed credible</b> concerning the <b>flasks</b> containing it?
The Gemara answers: <b>Rather,</b> it is referring <b>to empty</b> flasks that had contained <b>sacrificial</b> food <b>during the rest of the days of the year,</b> i.e., during all the days of the year, for an <i>am ha’aretz</i> is deemed credible with regard to sacrificial food throughout the year, yet he is not deemed credible with regard to its flask once the food has been removed, <b>and</b> it is referring as well <b>to</b> flasks <b>full of <i>teruma</i></b> wine <b>during the period of the winepress,</b> when they are deemed credible with regard to the <i>teruma</i> itself, but not the vessels.
The Gemara asks a question: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna: With regard to <b>jugs of wine and jugs of oil that are mingled [<i>meduma</i>],</b> <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are deemed credible during the period of the winepress and the olive press, and seventy days before the winepress. The Gemara asks: <b>What, is it not so that mingled</b> means that there is a mixture <b>of <i>teruma</i></b> oil or wine in the jug, and yet the mishna states that <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are deemed credible with regard to the jugs? This would appear to contradict the ruling of the <i>baraita</i> that they are not deemed credible with regard to flasks containing <i>teruma</i>. The Gemara answers: <b>In the school of Rabbi Ḥiyya they say</b> that the mishna in fact is referring to ordinary oil or wine <b>mingled with sacrificial</b> wine or oil, so that it contains a certain amount of sacrificial liquid.
The Gemara asks: <b>And does</b> the concept of <i>meduma</i> <b>apply to sacrificial</b> foods at all? This term <i>meduma</i> applies only to <i>teruma</i>, not to sacrificial items. In <b>the school of Rabbi Elai they say</b> that the <i>halakha</i> of the mishna is stated <b>with regard to one who is keeping his</b> wine <b><i>tevel</i>,</b> i.e., produce from which the requisite <i>teruma</i> and tithes have not yet been separated, <b>in purity,</b> because he intends <b>to take</b> wine for <b>libations from it.</b> It is therefore in a sense a mixture of sacrificial food, <i>teruma</i> and non-sacred food all in one, and accordingly the term <i>meduma</i> is applicable. The mishna is teaching that since the <i>am ha’aretz</i> is deemed credible with regard to the sacrificial food in this mixture, he is also deemed credible with regard to the <i>teruma</i> and the jugs.
§ It is taught in the mishna that apart from the period of the winepress itself, <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are also deemed credible <b>seventy days before</b> the period of <b>the winepress. Abaye said:</b> You can <b>learn from here,</b> incidental to the laws of purity, that <b>the law is that a tenant</b> farmer in a vineyard <b>must make the effort</b> of acquiring <b>flasks</b> for the wine <b>seventy days before</b> the time of <b>the winepress,</b> for the mishna considers this amount of time the period of preparation for the pressing of grapes.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>From Modi’im and inward</b> toward Jerusalem, i.e., in the area surrounding Jerusalem, up to the distance of the town of Modi’im, which is fifteen <i>mil</i> from Jerusalem, all potters, including <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, <b>are deemed credible with regard to</b> the purity of <b>earthenware vessels</b> that they have produced. Because these places supplied earthenware vessels for the people in Jerusalem, the Sages did not decree impurity for them. <b>From Modi’im and outward,</b> however, <b>they are not deemed credible.</b> The details of this ruling are specified: <b>How so? A potter who sells pots,</b> if he <b>entered within Modi’im</b> from outside it, although <b>the potter, and the pots, and the customers</b> were all previously located outside Modi’im, where he is not deemed credible with regard to purity, <b>he is</b> now <b>deemed credible.</b> And the opposite is true of the opposite case: If the same person who was deemed credible inside <b>left</b> the boundaries of Modi’im, <b>he is no</b> longer <b>deemed credible.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> A <i>tanna</i> <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>Modi’im</b> itself, <b>there are times</b> that it is considered <b>like inside</b> the perimeter surrounding Jerusalem, and <b>there are times</b> that it is considered <b>like outside</b> that perimeter. <b>How so?</b> If <b>a potter is leaving</b> the perimeter <b>and a <i>ḥaver</i> is entering</b> it, and they meet in Modi’im, it is considered <b>like inside,</b> and the <i>ḥaver</i> may purchase the jugs. However, if <b>both are entering</b> the perimeter

Daf 26a

<b>or both are leaving</b> it, it is considered <b>like outside</b> the perimeter and the <i>ḥaver</i> may not acquire vessels from him. The reason is that if they are both entering the perimeter they can easily wait until they are inside and then conduct the transaction, and if they are both leaving they should have completed the deal beforehand, and the <i>ḥaver</i> may not make up for this lapse by doing so now.
<b>Abaye said: We, too, learn</b> this in the mishna. For it is taught there: <b>A potter who was selling pots and entered within the Modi’im</b> area is deemed credible, which indicates that <b>the</b> only <b>reason</b> he is deemed credible is <b>that</b> he is <b>inside</b> the <b>Modi’im</b> area, <b>thus</b> implying that in <b>Modi’im itself he is not deemed credible.</b> But now <b>say the latter clause</b> of the mishna: If he <b>left he is not deemed credible, thus</b> implying that in <b>Modi’im itself he is deemed credible,</b> which contradicts the previous inference. <b>Rather,</b> must one <b>not conclude from</b> the mishna the following distinction: <b>Here,</b> in the latter clause, it is referring <b>to a potter</b> who is <b>leaving and a <i>ḥaver</i></b> who is <b>entering,</b> in which case he is deemed credible; and <b>there,</b> in the first clause, it is referring <b>to</b> a situation <b>where they are both leaving or both entering,</b> in which case he is not deemed credible. Consequently, both inferences from the mishna are upheld. The Gemara concludes: Indeed, <b>learn from here</b> that this is the case.
§ A <i>tanna</i> <b>taught</b> in the <i>Tosefta</i> (3:33): All people, including <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, <b>are deemed credible</b> with regard to purity from Modi’im and inward only <b>with regard to small earthenware vessels,</b> and they may be used <b>for sacrificial</b> food. Since these small vessels were needed by all, the Sages deemed the <i>amei ha’aretz</i> credible concerning them. The <i>amora’im</i> discussed the meaning of the term small vessels. <b>Reish Lakish said: It is</b> speaking of those vessels <b>that can be picked up in one hand,</b> but no larger. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Even if they cannot be picked up in one hand,</b> they can still be called small vessels.
<b>Reish Lakish said</b> further: <b>They taught</b> in the <i>baraita</i> <b>only</b> that <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are deemed credible with regard to <b>empty</b> vessels, <b>but</b> if they are <b>full</b> of liquid they are <b>not</b> deemed credible. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Even</b> if the jugs are <b>full, and even</b> if <b>his garment [<i>apikarsuto</i>] is inside</b> the vessel, the Sages were not concerned about impurity, as they did not apply their decree to such vessels at all. <b>And Rava said: And Rabbi Yoḥanan concedes with regard to the liquids themselves</b> in the vessel <b>that they are impure,</b> for although the Sages declared the vessels to be pure they did not waive the decree that liquids touched by <i>amei ha’aretz</i> are impure. <b>And do not be perplexed</b> by this apparent contradiction, <b>for</b> there is a similar <i>halakha</i> in a case of an earthenware <b>pitcher full of liquid</b> in a room with a corpse and the pitcher is tightly sealed with another earthenware vessel of an <i>am ha’aretz</i>, where the <i>halakha</i> is that the <b>pitcher is impure</b> with a <b>seven</b>-day <b>impurity, while the liquids</b> remain <b>pure.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of <i>amei ha’aretz</i> tax <b>collectors who entered a house</b> to collect items for a tax, <b>and similarly thieves who returned the vessels</b> they had stolen, <b>they are deemed credible when they say: We did not touch</b> the rest of the objects in the house, and those items remain pure. <b>And in Jerusalem</b> all people, even <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, <b>are deemed credible with regard to sacrificial</b> food throughout the year, <b>and during a pilgrimage Festival</b> they are deemed credible <b>even with regard to <i>teruma</i>.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from a different mishna (<i>Teharot</i> 7:6): If <i>amei ha’aretz</i> tax <b>collectors entered a house, the entire house is impure.</b> The Gemara answers: It is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>that</b> mishna is referring to a situation <b>where there is a gentile with them,</b> in which case they conduct a thorough search in the whole house, and certainly will have touched everything; whereas <b>this</b> mishna deals with a case <b>when there is no gentile with them,</b> and their claim not to have touched anything is therefore accepted. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Teharot</i> 7:6): <b>If there is a gentile with</b> the tax collectors, <b>they are deemed credible</b> if they were <b>to say: We did not enter</b> the house at all; <b>but they are not deemed credible</b> if they were <b>to say: We entered</b> the house <b>but did not touch</b> its vessels.
The Gemara raises a question: <b>And when there is a gentile with them, what of it?</b> Why does this affect the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan and Rabbi Elazar</b> disputed this issue. <b>One said: The fear of the gentile,</b> who is their senior, <b>is upon them,</b> for they are afraid he might punish them. <b>And one said: The fear of the kingdom,</b> i.e., the government, <b>is upon them,</b> as the gentile might report them to the authorities if they do not carry out a thorough search. The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the practical difference <b>between them?</b> The Gemara responds: <b>The</b> practical difference <b>between them</b> is the case of <b>a gentile who is not important,</b> i.e., he does not have senior authority. In that case they are not afraid of him personally, but there is still concern that he might report them to the government authorities.
§ It is taught in the mishna: <b>And similarly thieves who returned vessels</b> are deemed credible. <b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from the following mishna (<i>Teharot</i> 7:6): Concerning <b>the thieves who entered a house, only the place where the feet of the thieves had trodden is impure.</b> The implication is that all the vessels of the section of the house where they had entered are impure, and they are not deemed credible if they say that they did not touch a particular item. <b>Rav Pinḥas said in the name of Rav:</b> The mishna here is referring to a case <b>where</b> the thieves <b>repented,</b> which is why they are deemed credible, whereas the mishna in <i>Teharot</i> is referring to a case in which the thieves did not repent. The Gemara comments: The language of the mishna <b>is also precise, as it teaches:</b> Thieves <b>who returned vessels,</b> which indicates that they repented and made restoration willingly. The Gemara concludes: Indeed, <b>learn from here</b> that this is the case.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And in Jerusalem</b> all people <b>are deemed credible with regard to sacrificial</b> food. A <i>tanna</i> <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>They are deemed credible</b> even <b>with regard to large earthenware vessels for sacrificial</b> food, and not only small ones. <b>And why</b> did the Sages exhibit <b>so much</b> leniency, waiving their regular decrees of impurity within Jerusalem for large vessels and all the way to Modi’im for small vessels? Because there is a principle that potters’ <b>kilns may not be made in Jerusalem,</b> in order to preserve the quality of the air in the city. It is therefore necessary to bring in earthenware vessels from outside the city, and consequently the Sages were lenient concerning such utensils.
§ It was taught in the mishna: <b>And during a pilgrimage Festival</b> they are deemed credible <b>even with regard to <i>teruma</i>.</b> The Gemara poses a question: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived, i.e., that there is a difference between Festival days and other periods? <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: The verse states</b> concerning the incident of the concubine in Gibeah: <b>“And all the men of Israel gathered to the city, like one man, united [<i>ḥaverim</i>]”</b> (Judges 20:11). This verse is interpreted to teach that whenever the entire people of Israel gathers together in a single place, <b>the Torah makes,</b> i.e., considers, <b>all of them <i>ḥaverim</i>.</b> The final word of the phrase, <i>ḥaverim</i>, is a reference to the members of a group dedicated to scrupulous observance of mitzvot, as the term is used by the Sages.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of <b>one who opens his barrel</b> of wine for public sale, <b>and</b> similarly <b>one who starts</b> selling <b>his dough during the time of the pilgrimage Festival,</b> and these items perforce come into contact with <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> Since the food was pure, despite its contact with <i>amei ha’aretz</i>, when he began selling it, <b>he may finish</b> selling it in a state of purity even after the Festival, and there is no concern about the contact that has been made by <i>amei ha’aretz</i> during the Festival. <b>But the Rabbis say: He may not finish</b> selling it.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Ami and Rabbi Yitzḥak Nappaḥa were</b> once <b>sitting in the courtyard of Rabbi Yitzḥak Nappaḥa. One of them opened</b> the discussion <b>and said: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>with regard to</b> the possibility of <b>him leaving</b> his wine <b>for another,</b> subsequent <b>pilgrimage Festival</b> and continuing to sell it at that point? Although according to the Rabbis one may not continue selling it once the Festival has concluded, may he leave the barrel aside until the next Festival, at which point it would once again be able to be sold in purity?
<b>The other</b> Sage <b>said to him: Everyone’s hand has touched it, and</b> yet <b>you are saying that</b> perhaps <b>he may leave it for another pilgrimage Festival</b> and then sell it in purity? How could such a possibility even be considered? <b>He said</b> back <b>to him: Is that to say</b> that <b>until now,</b> throughout the Festival, <b>everyone’s hand was not touching it?</b> It was permitted during the Festival despite the fact that everyone was touching it; apparently, their touching did not render it impure at all. <b>He said to him: How can</b> these cases <b>be compared? Granted, until now, the Merciful One declares pure the impurity of the <i>am ha’aretz</i> during the Festival,</b> and consequently his impurity is disregarded, <b>but now</b> that the Festival has passed, the touch of an <i>am ha’aretz</i> <b>is</b> once again considered <b>impure.</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> this dispute between <i>amora’im</i> is <b>parallel to</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>. For it is taught</b> in <b>one</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>He may leave it for another pilgrimage Festival</b> and then continue to sell it. <b>And it was taught in a different</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>He may not leave it for another Festival. What, is it not</b> so that this very issue <b>is</b> a dispute between these two <b><i>tanna’im</i>,</b> the authors of these two <i>baraitot</i>?
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>No,</b> it is possible that <b>this</b> <i>baraita</i>, <b>which teaches</b> that <b>he may leave it,</b> follows the opinion, cited in the mishna, of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who allows the wine seller to finish selling his wine after the Festival, <b>whereas that</b> <i>baraita</i>, <b>which teaches</b> that <b>he may not leave it,</b> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who prohibit him to finish it. The Gemara questions this conclusion: <b>And</b> how can <b>you understand</b> it that way? <b>Didn’t Rabbi Yehuda say he may finish</b> it after the Festival? Consequently, there would be no need for him to leave it for another Festival. <b>Rather,</b> say as follows: <b>This</b> <i>baraita</i>, <b>which teaches</b> that <b>he may not leave it,</b> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, whereas that</b> <i>baraita</i>, <b>which teaches</b> that <b>he may leave it,</b> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis. And what is</b> the meaning of the statement: <b>He may not leave it</b> for another Festival? It means <b>that he has no need to leave it</b> for another Festival, as Rabbi Yehuda maintains he can finish selling it in purity immediately.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Once the pilgrimage Festival has passed by,</b> the priests <b>pass</b> all the vessels of <b>the Temple courtyard</b> through a process of <b>purification,</b> since they were touched by <i>am ha’aretz</i> priests during the Festival. If <b>the Festival passed by into a Friday,</b> i.e., if the Festival ended on Thursday night, <b>they would not pass</b> the vessels through the purification process on that day, <b>due to the honor of Shabbat,</b> in order to give the priests time to prepare the requirements of Shabbat. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> They do <b>not even</b> purify them <b>on Thursday,</b> in the event that the Festival ended on Wednesday night, because <b>the priests are not free</b> to do so.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> A <i>tanna</i> <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>, in explanation of Rabbi Yehuda’s words: The priests do not purify the vessels of the Temple courtyard on Thursday, <b>as the priests are not free from removing the ashes.</b> During the Festival days a large quantity of ash would accumulate on the altar, due to the large number of offerings brought at that time. Because they would not remove the ashes on the Festival itself, they would have to remove a very large amount afterward. Consequently, all the priests were kept busy with this task upon the conclusion of the Festival, which did not leave them with enough time to deal with other matters.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>How do they pass</b> all the vessels of the Temple <b>courtyard</b> through a process of <b>purification? They immerse the vessels that were in the Temple. And they say to</b> the <i>am ha’aretz</i> priests who served in the Temple during the Festival: <b>Be careful</b>

Daf 26b

<b>that you not touch the table</b> of the shewbread. If you defile it by touching it, it would need to be removed for immersion, and this would lead to the temporary suspension of the mitzva of the shewbread, which had to be on the table at all times.
The mishna continues: <b>All the vessels that were in the Temple had second and third</b> substitute vessels, <b>so that if the first ones became impure they could bring the second ones in their place. All the vessels that were in the Temple required immersion</b> after the Festival, <b>apart from the golden altar and the bronze altar, because they are</b> considered <b>like the ground</b> and therefore, like land itself, not susceptible to impurity. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer. And the Rabbis say:</b> It is <b>because they are coated.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> A <i>tanna</i> <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that they would say to the <i>am ha’aretz</i> priests: <b>Be careful lest you touch the table,</b> as explained above, <b>or the candelabrum,</b> as the Gemara will explain. The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> regarding <b>the <i>tanna</i> of our</b> mishna, <b>what is the reason he did not teach</b> that they were instructed not to touch the <b>candelabrum</b> as well? The Gemara answers: <b>With regard to the table it is written:</b> “Shewbread before Me <b>always”</b> (Exodus 25:30), indicating that the table holding the shewbread must always be in its place, whereas <b>with regard to the candelabrum it is not written “always,”</b> and therefore it can be removed for immersing.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> regarding <b>the other</b> <i>tanna</i>, in the <i>baraita</i>, why does he include the candelabrum? The Gemara answers: <b>Since it is written: “And</b> you shall set the table without the veil <b>and the candelabrum opposite the table”</b> (Exodus 26:35), indicating that the candelabrum must always be placed opposite the table; <b>it is as though it is written “always” with regard to</b> the candelabrum as well. <b>And the other</b> <i>tanna</i>, in the mishna, who does not object to removing the candelabrum for immersion, would reply: <b>That</b> verse <b>comes</b> only <b>to establish a place for</b> the candelabrum, to describe where it must be positioned, but it does not mean to say that it must be opposite the table at all times.
The Gemara poses a question concerning the requirement to keep <i>amei ha’aretz</i> away from the table: <b>And let us derive it,</b> i.e., let it be established, that it is not necessary to take care against contact with the table, as it is incapable of contracting ritual impurity. This is <b>because it is a wooden vessel designated to rest</b> in a fixed place, <b>and</b> the <i>halakha</i> is that <b>any</b> large, <b>wooden vessel designated to rest</b> in a fixed place <b>cannot become impure. What is the reason</b> for this <i>halakha</i>? Since wooden vessels and sacks are juxtaposed in the verse describing their impurity (Leviticus 11:32), <b>we require</b> a wooden vessel <b>to be similar to a sack</b> in order to be capable of contracting impurity, in the following manner: <b>Just as a sack is carried</b> when it is both <b>full and empty, so too any</b> wooden vessel that is <b>carried full and empty</b> can contract impurity, as opposed to vessels, such as the table, that are designated to rest in a fixed place. The table should therefore not be susceptible to impurity at all.
The Gemara answers: The table <b>too is</b> in fact <b>carried full and empty, in accordance with</b> the words <b>of Reish Lakish. For Reish Lakish said: What is</b> the meaning of that <b>which is written:</b> “And you shall set them in two rows, six in a row, <b>upon the pure table”</b> (Leviticus 24:6)? The words “pure table” teach <b>by inference that it is</b> capable of becoming <b>impure,</b> and therefore the Torah warns us to make sure it is pure when the twelve loaves of bread are placed there.
<b>And why</b> indeed is the table susceptible to ritual impurity, being that <b>it is a wooden vessel designated to rest</b> in a fixed place <b>and</b> should therefore <b>not</b> be <b>susceptible to impurity? Rather,</b> this verse <b>teaches that they</b> would <b>lift</b> the table with the shewbread on it <b>to display the shewbread to the pilgrims</b> standing in the Temple courtyard, as it was prohibited for Israelites to enter the Sanctuary, where the table stood, <b>and they</b> would <b>say to them: Behold your affection before God,</b> Who performs a perpetual miracle with the bread, for when <b>it is removed</b> from the table on Shabbat it is just as fresh <b>as</b> when <b>it was arranged</b> on the previous Shabbat. <b>As Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi said: A great miracle was performed with the shewbread: As its</b> condition during its <b>arrangement, so was its</b> condition during its <b>removal, as it is stated: “To place hot bread on the day when it was taken away”</b> (I Samuel 21:7), indicating that it was as hot on the day of its removal as it was on the day when it was placed.
The Gemara asks another question: <b>Let us derive</b> this fact, i.e., that the table can contract ritual impurity, not because it is portable but <b>due to</b> its golden <b>coating. For didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Kelim</i> 22:1): Concerning <b>a table and a <i>dulpaki</i> that</b> some of its surface <b>became broken off, or that one coated with marble,</b> i.e., stone not being susceptible to impurity: If <b>he left on them a place</b> on the surface that remained unbroken or uncoated, big enough <b>for placing cups, it</b> remains <b>susceptible to impurity</b> as a wooden vessel. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> It must have an unbroken and uncoated <b>place</b> big enough <b>for placing pieces</b> of meat and bread as well in order to maintain susceptibility to impurity as a wooden vessel. It is clear from this mishna that if a table is completely coated with stone it is not susceptible to impurity, showing that the status of a vessel follows its external coating, not its main material. The Temple table, which was coated with gold, should have the status of a metal vessel.
The Gemara proposes a possible answer: <b>And if you would say</b> that <b>acacia wood,</b> from which the Temple table was made, <b>is different, as</b> it is an <b>important,</b> valuable kind of wood <b>and is</b> therefore <b>not nullified</b> by a coating, <b>this works out well according to Reish Lakish, who said: They taught</b> that a wooden vessel is nullified by its coating <b>only with regard to vessels</b> made of cheap <b><i>akhselag</i></b> wood <b>which comes from overseas, but</b> vessels made of expensive <b><i>masmi</i></b> wood <b>are not nullified</b> by a coating. According to this opinion it is <b>fine,</b> for we can say that the acacia wood of the table is also not nullified by its golden coating. <b>But according to Rabbi Yoḥanan, who said: Even</b> expensive <b><i>masmi</i> vessels are also nullified</b> by a coating, <b>what is there to say?</b>
The Gemara proposes another possible answer: <b>And if you would say</b> that the mishna is not applicable because <b>here</b> in the mishna the wood is nullified by its coating because it is speaking <b>of a fixed coating,</b> whereas <b>there</b> in the case of the Temple table the golden <b>coating</b> is <b>not fixed</b> onto the wood, this is impossible. For <b>didn’t Reish Lakish inquire of Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> Does this law that vessels follow their coating deal only <b>with a fixed coating or</b> even <b>with a coating that is not fixed?</b> And he asked him further: Does it deal only <b>with</b> a coating <b>that covers the</b> table’s <b>rim</b> as well as the table itself, <b>or</b> even <b>with one that does not cover its rim?</b>
And Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to him</b> in response: <b>It is not different</b> if <b>it is a fixed coating and it is not different</b> if <b>it is a coating that is not fixed;</b> and <b>it is not different</b> if the coating <b>covers</b> the table’s <b>rim and it is not different</b> if <b>it does not cover its rim.</b> Therefore, since the coating always determines the status of the vessel, the Temple table, with its gold coating, should be susceptible to impurity. <b>Rather,</b> we must say a different explanation as to why the coating does not make the table susceptible to impurity: The <b>table is different</b>

Daf 27a

<b>because the Merciful One called it: “Wood,” as it is written</b> with regard to the table of the shewbread: <b>“The altar, three cubits high, and its length two cubits, was of wood, and so its corners, its length, and its walls were also of wood, and he said to me: This is the table that is before the Lord”</b> (Ezekiel 41:22). This verse indicates that even though the table was coated, its identity as a wooden vessel was preserved, which means that, like all wooden vessels, it would not be susceptible to impurity were it not for the fact that they took it out to show to the pilgrims.
As the Gemara has cited the above verse, it clarifies a puzzling aspect of it: The verse <b>began with the</b> word <b>“altar” and ended with the</b> word <b>“table,”</b> both words describing the same item. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish both say</b> the following exposition: <b>When the Temple is standing the altar atones for a person; now</b> that the Temple has been destroyed, it is <b>a person’s table</b> that <b>atones for him,</b> for his feeding of needy guests atones for his sins.
§ The mishna taught: <b>All the vessels that were in the Temple had second</b> and third substitute vessels, <b>etc.</b> All the vessels that were in the Temple required immersion, apart from the golden altar and the bronze altar, because they are considered like the ground. The Gemara cites the relevant sources: <b>The bronze altar</b> is considered like the ground, <b>as it written</b> concerning this altar: <b>“An altar of earth you shall make for Me”</b> (Exodus 20:21). <b>The golden altar</b> is considered like the ground, <b>as it is written: “The candelabrum and the altars”</b> (Numbers 3:31). The plural word “altars” indicates that <b>the</b> two <b>altars</b> of the Temple, the bronze one and the golden one, <b>are compared to each other,</b> teaching that just as the bronze altar is like the ground in that it cannot contract impurity, the same applies to the golden altar.
§ According to the mishna, Rabbi Eliezer maintains that the altars are pure because they are like the ground, <b>and the Rabbis say:</b> It is <b>because they are coated.</b> The mishna seems to be saying that the Rabbis are offering a different reason for the altars not being susceptible to impurity, namely, that they are coated. The Gemara is puzzled by this: <b>On the contrary, since they are coated</b> with gold or bronze that is a reason that they should <b>contract impurity,</b> as the metal coating makes the entire altar considered as a metal vessel, as stated above, and metal is susceptible to impurity. The Gemara answers: <b>Say</b> and emend the mishna text as follows: <b>And the Rabbis</b> disagree with Rabbi Eliezer altogether, and <b>declare</b> the altars <b>to be susceptible to impurity, because they are coated.</b>
<b>And if you wish, say</b> that our text of the mishna is correct, and we should understand that <b>the Rabbis were saying</b> their statement in response <b>to Rabbi Eliezer: What is your reasoning</b> for stating that the altars are not susceptible to impurity because they are like the ground? Why didn’t you say simply that they are wooden vessels fixed in one place? For that is sufficient reason for them not to be susceptible to impurity. Is it <b>because</b> they <b>are coated</b> with metal, and therefore they would be considered metal vessels rather than wooden ones, and susceptible to impurity, were it not for the fact that they are considered like the ground?
This is a mistake, for the coating is not important, and <b>their coating is nullified</b> and considered subordinate <b>to them,</b> so that they are indeed considered wooden vessels in a fixed place and therefore not susceptible to impurity. Although generally the status of a vessel does follow its coating, the Temple table and its altars are exceptions, as derived from the verse in Ezekiel cited above (41:22). There is therefore no need to mention that they are compared to the ground. Since these altars are made of wood they do not contract impurity, irrespective of whether or not they are attached to the ground.
§ Apropos the coating of the altar, the Gemara cites an Aggadic teaching: <b>Rabbi Abbahu said</b> that <b>Rabbi Elazar said:</b> The <b>fire of Gehenna has no power over Torah scholars.</b> This can be derived by <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from the salamander [<i>salamandra</i>],</b> a creature created out of fire and immune to its effects, and whose blood is fireproof: <b>If a salamander, which is</b> merely <b>a product of fire,</b> and nevertheless when <b>one anoints</b> his body <b>with its blood, fire has no power over him, all the more so</b> should fire not have any power over <b>Torah scholars, whose entire bodies are fire, as it is written: “Surely My words are as fire, says the Lord”</b> (Jeremiah 23:29), and the words of Torah become part of the Torah scholars’ very bodies.
<b>Reish Lakish said:</b> The <b>fire of Gehenna has no power over the sinners of Israel</b> either. This can be derived by <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from the golden altar: If the golden altar, which</b> has <b>on it</b> a coating that is <b>no more than the thickness of a gold dinar,</b> and which has incense burning on it <b>for many years</b> and yet <b>fire has no power over it,</b> as the gold miraculously remained undamaged, <b>all the more so</b> should immunity from fire be granted to <b>the sinners of Israel, who are filled with good deeds as a pomegranate</b> is full of seeds, <b>as it is written: “Your temples [<i>rakatekh</i>] are like a pomegranate split open”</b> (Song of Songs 4:3), which is to be expounded as follows: <b>Do not read</b> this word as <b><i>rakatekh</i>, rather</b> read it as <b><i>reikanin shebakh</i>,</b> meaning the empty, worthless people among you; even these people are as full of good deeds as a pomegranate is full of seeds.
